The Text, Please. Just the Text.

I call this page The Text. It is a series of short posts on a range of subjects. Subjects cover coding and technical matters, food and lifestyle, family and activities, drafts of ideas for further exploration, and more. I include a sprinkle of personal reflections and commentary.

Peruse the ToC to skip around. Use the RSS feed to monitor new posts.

On Side Effects

June 20, 2025

The Year of the Linux Desktop (YLD) is a running joke since at least the early 2000s. It's a running joke because Linux (any distro) has only ever had a few percentage points of the desktop market, not counting Chrome OS and Android, both of which are Linux or Linux-kernel based.

Since the early 2000s, a number of events have created hope that this year (insert whatever year) would be the Year of the Linux Desktop. In 2025, two main events are renewing hope. The first event is connected to the new hardware requirements for Windows 11 and Microsoft's end of support for Windows 10. This means that many users will have to buy completely new hardware to run Windows 11, instead of being able to upgrade their current machines. Since Linux doesn't have draconian hardware requirements, the hope is that this will spur users to replace Windows 10 with their Linux distro of choice (i.e., the side effect) rather than replacing their machines to get Windows 11. (It would also be good not to dump a bunch of perfectly usable machines to the trash heap.)

The second event is connected to Trump's executive order against members of the International Criminal Court. The order meant that U.S. companies, including Microsoft, were barred from providing services, such as email, Word, and other services, to the ICC (NY Times, 2025). At least one ICC member was then locked out of their Outlook email. The outcome of this (and other events) is that various European governments are becoming increasingly worried about their digital sovereignty. For example, the Denmark government is supposedly migrating to Linux as their OS and to LibreOffice for their office suite software. (Note: Denmark may not be dropping Windows, per the OMG Ubuntu article, but other sources state they are. So who knows.) Anyway, if true, then Thanks, Trump.

The side effects here are that these events might help result in the YLD. Personally, after so many this is the Year of the Linux Desktop claims, I am doubtful. Microsoft has done a stellar job of maintaining dominance. But, on the other hand, we used to say that same thing about Disney and the never ending copyright extensions acts; then Mickey Mouse finally entered the public domain last year.

The Web Sucks: Colors Part II

June 17, 2025

I ditched the method I described yesterday because there were a few problems. First, although I strongly liked the colors I set in Firefox's contrast settings, the dark background color I used washes out substantial parts of a site. I can live with that, but then other issues surfaced. E.g., we use the Canvas LMS for course management, and with the contrast color settings in effect there are something like 15 borders drawn around a discussion forum post. Many of these borders cross through text, too. And so Canvas becomes unusable because content becomes unreadable.

I found and tried the Stylus Firefox add-on, which lets you add your own CSS style sheet and make global, personalized modifications to sites based on that, but this didn't work. Basically, I created a stylesheet for Stylus that focused on basic HTML elements. It turns out that a lot of websites hardly use basic HTML elements at all. I inspected the HTML of a few (like Canvas and nytimes.com) and saw total awfulness. Like, HTML heading elements are used to mark up regular paragraphs. Wth, people.

I would love to use a command line/text-mode browser more. I prefer w3m, but like most text-mode browsers, it doesn't support JavaScript (JS). Websites have become way too dependent on JS, and many are simply inaccessible without a browser that supports it. Browsh.sh, a command-line browser, supports JS, but it has other problems with rendering.

I might try to tinker with Stylus in the future, but in the meantime, I've settled on Gammastep to adjust monitor settings, like color temperatures and gamma correction. This has been a bit helpful. I use the following gammastep command to adjust color temperature and gamma correction based on my current location (-l LAT:LONG),

gammastep -l 38.04:-84.5 -t 5700:3600 -g 0.8 &

Despite trying to make screen glare go away (I see my eye doctor in a few weeks and will discuss solutions with her, too), I'm mostly disappointed with how awful the web is today. Even when websites so-called work with w3m or like, I still have to page down a couple of hundreds of lines (dozens of sign up links, navigation links, etc.) to get to content.

Colors

June 16, 2025

For all the decades that I've used a terminal, I've preferred white text on a black background for its colors. Lately, my (aging) eyes haven't enjoyed this so much, and recently I found that a dark blue background works much better. I still use white for most text, but I use a more vibrant blue for some text accents.

This color combination has worked so well that I decided to use it in my browser. I tried a dark mode plugin first, but the contrast between white and black (even after adjusting a bunch of settings) didn't help with this apparent glare that bothers my eyes. To fix this, in Firefox's settings, under Language and Appearance, I changed the background to the dark blue I use in my terminal, unvisited links to a vibrant red, and visited links to a soft yellow. It's not perfect because these settings wash out all other colors. For example, I can't see the colors that I link to in this post; but in the meantime, it's a nice change.

A Short Trip

June 13, 2025

The kids and I took a short vacation to Cincinnati, and we had a lot of fun. We stayed in a church that had been converted into a home, with a residence for guests, and that had a public area for indoor boulder/rock climbing. All three of my kids loved it, and I may have to investigate the local climbing gym so that they can continue to practice.

I was amazed by what they were able to climb in the few days we were there. They'd spend hours, individually and collectively, figuring out how to climb a certain segment, and they pursued those lines with extreme focus. It was an absolute delight to watch their their determination in play and to see their hard work pay off. What a blast!

Digital Housekeeping

June 05, 2025

One of my goals this summer is to clean the web house, so to speak, and do a bunch of digital housekeeping. I've already finished a lot in the last week. For example, I'm ditching my Dropbox pro account before it renews and have moved my files to two backup disks. One disk contains my main files, and the other disk contains photos and video. I still need to go through and organize some of that, but the major work is done.

I've fixed up this site, too. I have a Notes section (link on homepage), and moved a bunch of pages there and reorganized that section's main page.

This summer I might also see how feasible it is to move to FreeBSD as my main operating system. Linux is getting messy itself. In the past, we'd primarily install software through the distribution's package management system, but now I'm compiling from source more often, using Flatpak, Snap, AppImage (sometimes), Cargo, etc. I have executables in too many different paths, and it's getting confusing. It's like a modern day version of dependency hell. So, once I'm done with my digital housecleaning, I might see if I can migrate to a saner system. One of the nicest things about FreeBSD, and the other BSDs, is how elegant it is.

I Do and Not I Am

June 02, 2025

Some years back I became sour on the statement "I am a professor" and switched instead to the phrase "I work as a professor." I got hung up on the work that "to be" does in that sentence and wondered why I would want to assign that word to my job's title.

It might have something to do with parenting and how I think about being a parent. I am a parent and being a parent is part of my essence. It's understated to say that it's much more important than the job I do. I can imagine switching careers someday, but I will be a parent for as long as I exist. I can conclude then that to be something thus means to me that I will always be that thing. And someday I may not be a professor.

Perhaps then my issue with stating "I am a professor" is that although I'm twelve years into it, or even eighteen years if we count working towards it as a graduate student, it's never clicked for me as a thing that I am because I can, at the very least, entertain the idea that it may not be what I will always be. Interestingly, it felt liberating when I first realized that and made the conscious switch to "I work as ...."

Alternatively, I worked as a cook for quite a long time before I went to graduate school, and I used to say "I am a cook." A part of me thinks that I am still a cook even though it's not something I do professionally anymore. Cooking was more than a job for me. It was very much a form of service and even prayer. I adopted this view because of Thomas Merton, the Cistercian monk, who made me rethink how and what it means to pray. And because of food writers like MFK Fisher, who made me think deeply about what it means for people to break bread with each other. What Fisher wrote made cooking for people feel noble, as service feels noble. Cooking still feels like service and prayer even when I do it at home.

Also, I must say that even though I do not work as a cook anymore, some of the people I worked with as a cook, when I was a cook, still cook with me in spirit. That is, I never cook alone, it feels like, and I think that is an important part of why I feel like I am still a cook.

If it doesn't feel right to say "I am a professor," but it does feel right to say "I am a parent" or "I am a cook," then this must mean that working as a professor feels somehow divorced from what I had in my mind being a professor was and is. Looking back, I think I had it in my mind that being a professor meant being a learner and a student. We might call these two things being a scholar, as in being someone who engages in learning and pursues study. Indeed, I think I am good with saying that I am a learner, that I am a student, and therefore, I am a scholar. These seem to be core parts of who I am, and in that context, it makes sense to say that I do these things professionally, and that I profess what I learn and study. In that sense, I can see making the claim "I am a professor" if I mean by that "I profess as a learner and a student."

But, I don't want to make that claim. It still feels like a job title, as something I do rather than am. My sentiments on this might be more an indictment of my sentiments on the state of higher education and on the day-to-day work of being a professor and on working with others who are professors. These contextual and day-to-day practical aspects often don't seem to have anything to do with being a learner or being a student.

I don't mean to romanticize a cook's life or being a cook. It can be an ugly job, and the people can be awful and self-serving, too. But it's mostly, for most cooks, an invisible job, just like parenting is. However, unlike being a cook (in many cases), and hopefully being a parent, the currency of faculty is reputation (ala Robert Merton, the sociologist of science and no relation to Thomas Merton), which means more often acting to serve one's own interests rather than others in order to grow one's reputation. As evidence of this, I think it's more common to hear people in my profession say that we are experts in some area rather than students, learners, or scholars in some area. But to claim expertise is a claim to authority over something. It is to bend a thing to our will, to establish our dominion over it, and to me that signals how people carve out their epistemological and political territory as professors. However, to align one's self as a learner and student is to submit to a kind of intellectual humility, to yield to one's intellectual limits and to the limits of knowledge. To claim one's self to be a learner and student is to acknowledge a more communal goal toward knowledge.

I know many faculty who are service-oriented, though, in spite of all odds. These are admirable people, and I respect them deeply.

But perhaps the fallacy of my life was to think that I ought to be a professor simply because I am forever a learner and a student. I.e., being a learner and a student does not necessarily entail being a professor. Being a professor, sadly, does not necessarily entail being a learner and a student, either. I'm hesitant to write that because of the recent, elevated, misguided, unethical attacks on higher education.

In any case, if I don't feel like I am a professor, then it must be because: it doesn't feel permanent, it doesn't feel communal, it doesn't feel like service, and it feels divorced from being scholarly.

Roller Skating is Not Like Bike Riding

June 01, 2025

I took my girlfriend on a roller skating date last night, and it was so fun. I think this was the first real skating experience I've had since I was a teen.

Getting back into skates is not like riding a bike, though. My guess is that riding a bike is easier because it involves fewer muscle groups, less coordination, and less focus, especially as we gain momentum on our bikes.

Roller skating, on the contrary, is quite the workout. It seemed to require so many more muscle groups, total body coordination, and much more focus when starting or relearning. I had to make constant whole body micro-adjustments while skating, which is totally not the case when riding. As evidence, my heart rate hovered in the vigorous range for 72% of the time I skated. I never get anywhere near that when riding, at least not when when riding casually. Anyway, it seems far more difficult to relearn to skate because so much more of the body is involved in skating.

I'm sure it becomes second nature after the skills have developed or returned, but even after nearly two hours of skating, I was barely getting into the zone. I think it'll take a couple more sessions before I start to feel decent at it.

Also, I want to say that I was completely in awe of the roller skating culture. People of all skills and ages were in the rink, and everyone was so tolerant if not welcoming of each other. And I saw groups of teens, young men mostly, skillfully skating synchronously together. It was beautiful to watch. I don't remember seeing anything like that when I skated at that age, when people, as I recall, seemed to (dumbly) take on a macho attitude about skating and everything else. This newer culture is so much more inspiring.

On Laughter

May 31, 2025

We can offend when we laugh or make humor at something tragic or sad. My guess is the offense seems to arise out of a sense, from others, that we lack empathy or don't feel sad about some thing. However, a sense of humor may also arise from a deeply religious, empathetic, and also existential perspective that life is both deeply meaningful (the tragic response to anguish) and deeply absurd (the comedic response to anguish).

For me, my sense of humor helps me fight, resist, not give up, and keep some kind of perspective that is above me.

Yet it's true that laughter can be a cruel, scornful response: e.g., Nietzsche's 'laughter of the herd'. Perhaps this is why laughter as a response to the tragic or awful can be misconstrued. For example, we've seen in recent years a number of critiques against comedians who punch down, and my guess is that this may inform a general response to laughter.

But it's also true that humor is a coping mechanism.

Anyway, I was thinking about this because I found a couple of passages I had highlighted long ago in my copy of Moby Dick:

Because a laugh's the wisest, easiest answer to all that's queer

and,

There are certain queer times and occasions in this strange mixed affair we call life when a man takes this whole universe for a vast practical joke

I suppose I've been thinking about this for a while.

Professional Cleaning

May 31, 2025

I bought my current phone about two years ago. It functions well, but it has had charging problems for the last few weeks, and I'd have to fiddle with the USB-C cable to get a secure connection and maintain a charge. When the problem first appeared, I'd only have to fiddle with the cable connection for a bit, but lately it required a bunch of fiddling to get a good connection. It was frustrating, and for a while I thought it'd get to a point where it wouldn't keep the connection and thus not charge at all.

After some quick web searching, I learned that the charging port was probably dirty. I tried to clean it myself with compressed air and (carefully) with a toothpick. Neither method did the trick.

Yesterday I brought it to a phone repair shop, and they were able to clean out the port. It took them a few minutes to clean it up. It was a $40 charge, which seems like a lot, but it solved the problem, and it's a lot cheaper than a new phone.

On the way out, I stopped at a used book store in that area and bought two books: one on learning how to program in C and the other a fantasy novel.

Sourdough Hydration and Starter Feeding

May 30, 2025

I was having a hard time with my sourdough bread recently, which would come out rather flat after baking. But after some testing, I figured it was probably the result of two things: dough hydration and starter feeding.

In the colder (and dryer) months of the year, I would feed my starter once after using it. Then I'd let it sit for a few hours so that it could eat, and I'd feed it again later in the day before placing it in the fridge. In the fridge state, the bacteria would consume the rest of the flour and water more slowly. This worked fine because during the colder months, the bacteria feeds more slowly overall, even when the house is heated.

However, this method hadn't been working well this spring. And I think this was because the starter would feed too quickly, and by the time I'd use it to make bread, it had overfed. It turns out that the starter should not be hungry when I use it. I can't remember if I knew this and had forgotten it or hadn't known this at all.

To fix this, I still feed it a first meal after making the dough, but then I immediately put it in the fridge. Then on bread making day, I pull it out of the fridge, feed it, and let it sit on the counter until it's had a chance to eat. I use it to make dough when it's doubled and is all bubbly.

The other issues is that I've noticed that the weather seems to effect the hydration levels. I've written before how the changing seasons effect how my sourdough bread rises. which might be more pronounced this spring because it's been especially rainy and humid. Thus the other change I made was during the dough making process. In the colder, dryer months, I had been using 300 grams of water to make the dough, but recently this also seemed to contribute to a flatter loaf. So the other change I made was to reduce the amount of water I'd add to the dough to 250 grams. The result has been so much better. The loaf rises higher and is less dense.

I first started making sourdough bread back in 2001, and my current starter is almost three years old now. Even after all this time, I'm still learning so much about this living organism.

Section Numbering in CSS

May 30, 2025

I added some section numbering to my CSS using the counter-reset and counter-increment CSS properties. I targeted my homepage with the following code, which adds outline style numbering to the <h2> and <h3> elements:

/* Adding outline numbering to homepage */

body #homepage {
    counter-reset: main_outline;
}

body #homepage h2::before {
  content: counter(main_outline) " ";
  counter-increment: main_outline;
}

body #homepage h2 {
  counter-reset: sub_outline;
}

body #homepage h3::before {
  content: counter(main_outline) "." counter(sub_outline) " ";
  counter-increment: sub_outline;
}

The above code should work in any browser.

For this page, though, I use <ol> with the reversed attribute for the table of contents. I wanted to mirror the numbering in the table of contents with the matching posts. For example, this post should be "#346" in the table of contents, and I want to add "Post 346: Title of Post" to the <h2> element for the actual post.

To do this, I used counter-reset: reversed(numbering); to target the <h2> elements used in each post. Unfortunately, neither Safari nor Chrome (the basis of most browsers these days), support the reversed keyword. So I used the @moz-document function to target the Firefox browser only. Without targeting Firefox only, the output is funky in other browsers. Anyway, the result is "Post 346: Title of Post" in Firefox. For those using other browsers, only the "Title of Post" is displayed.

/* Adding post numbering to The Text */
/* Applying to Firefox only: Chrome doesn't support the reversed() keyword yet in counter-reset */

@-moz-document url-prefix() {
  body #thetext {
    counter-reset: reversed(numbering);
  }
  
  body #thetext article > h2::before {
  counter-increment: numbering -1;
  content: "Post " counter(numbering) ": ";
  }
}

A Blank Canvas

May 22, 2025

Terminal or command-line based work is spartan looking by default, but people make great efforts to theme the experience. They do this by modifying their shell prompts and various terminal applications, including their text editors.

For example, many text editors support syntax highlighting. This enables the text editor to use different colors and formatting to display text. This can help when writing code because a text editor with syntax highlighting recognizes and highlights a programming language's keywords plus other aspects of the code. It may also highlight errors in the code. The same basic idea is used in word processors that highlight spelling errors or like. Thus, theming support is not merely eye candy but also enhances the user experience or UX.

Text editors can use color schemes (or themes) that use different colors and formatting to display text. I use the vim text editor, which comes with a default set of color schemes. The website vimcolorschemes provides a database of user contributed color schemes that others can use. Many of these are quite beautiful. Other text editors have their own theming support.

One of the first text editors I used was nvi, which doesn't support syntax highlighting. When I started using a text editor that did (vim), I was totally in awe and thought it was the coolest thing ever. In recent years, though, I've become much more spartan in my tastes and have occasionally searched the vimcolorschemes website for minimal color schemes that I can use.

The problem was that although there are lots of minimal color schemes on that site and elsewhere (like GitHub), none were minimal enough. They all used some kind of syntax highlighting for code and like plus various background and foreground colors.

To resolve this, I finally wrote my own color scheme file for Vim. I call it blank, and it's available at https://github.com/cseanburns/blank. It supports only what I think are the most functional aspects of syntax highlighting, which means it supports:

  • highlighting text (called visual mode in Vim)
  • highlighting matching parentheses
  • spelling errors
  • search terms

I also wanted it to support Markdown formatting. This means my Vim blank color scheme will render **bold** as bold, *italics* as italics, ~~strikethrough~~ as strikethrough, and [links](https://example.com) as links. But nothing more.

Importantly, the foreground and background color of the editor uses the same colors as my terminal, which is important to me.

I've been using this for a few days now, and I'm happy that I finally decided to roll my own.

Done with AI

May 21, 2025

I deleted my ChatGPT history and logged out. I didn't delete my account because it warns you that you can't recreate the account with the same login information, so I guess I'm hedging.

Part of me is going to miss it because I found it to be a nice conversationalist.

But the more I think about it, the more I realized that I like to work through ideas, etc on my own or with other people. Although given the latter, I lament that people, like me, are often too busy, too tired, or to something else to often engage in meaningful ways. That's the nice thing about gen AI: it's nearly always there and ready for a conversation.

Still, I prefer simpler times. The world feels increasingly chaotic, and I don't like it. I also don't like how dependent we've become on technology, most of which doesn't work well. I mean, I can't emphasize enough how awful most software is these days, for a variety of reasons.

Anyway, that's all beside the point. More than that, each new technology is one more technology to become addicted to, and those addictions primarily serve the interest of someone else's mega wealth.

I did not delete my sessions with and logout from ChatGPT because of it's impact on the environment and planet, although I've been thinking about that more. I find that argument a bit empty and that the people who make those kinds of arguments are likely streaming lots of videos, uploading lots of images, using the web a lot, buying new smartphones too often, etc etc. Those things bring with them a cost to the environment, too, and yet the anger about their impact never seemed to reach any kind of audible crescendo before.

On Amputations

May 18, 2025

Recently I used Gen AI to help clean up my .bashrc, .bash_functions, and .vimrc files. Yet, when I used the new versions of these files, it felt gross.

I think it felt this way because before ChatGPT, I'd tackle such challenges on my own, and I'd feel good, accomplished, and even self-improved as a result. After ChatGPT, it doesn't feel that way at all and those files didn't feel like my own. And that's important to me because I've worked on these files for decades. In short, these files have more than a functional purpose. Since they document my thinking and understanding over that time frame, they have an archaeological purpose to me. Writing them also involved having to think closely about my system and read documentation, and as such, they have a learning purpose. ChatGPT might aid in producing outputs, but if not used carefully, when we ditch the process of doing the work ourselves, we ditch the ability to learn and we corrupt the archaeology. (Fortunately, ChatGPT didn't do a great job with any of these files, which is interesting because the technology is well documented.)

I've been thinking a lot lately about the effects of generative AI on other cognitive abilities, too. Before mobile phones, I used to remember lots of phone numbers. Before smartphones and current mapping technologies, I used to know how to get around where I lived (or use a map). To this point, after 12 years of living where I live but using Google Maps a lot, I still have a fairly vague mental map of my city that's only recently getting better. Basically, in these two example, these specific technologies have made me dumber in specific ways.

Since generative AI has much more general capabilities, I'm now concerned that if I use generative AI too much, I'll lose something more general, and I'm not sure what I would gain as a result, besides making life a bit easier. And I'm a strong believer that life is better when it's at least kind of hard.

Socrates dished writing and its effects on memory. However, unlike Socrates, I think writing (and documentation) is important. Documentation helps us build new knowledge, and writing (text, code, etc) helps us think. Revising our writing (code, etc) is even more important, because that's when we really wrestle with structure, logic, meaning, and more. Other forms of expression (like art) have their own cognitive benefits, too. Basically, per Socrates, we may lose some ability to memorize when we write and that may alter how we think (but not thinking itself), but in these cases, the gains far outweigh a predominantly oral culture. If ChatGPT or like doesn't help me think, like writing and revising do, then I shouldn't use it, at the risk of losing the ability to think.

All this is to say that a recent post by Neal Stephenson wrote hit home hard:

Speaking of the effects of technology on individuals and society as a whole, Marshall McLuhan wrote that every augmentation is also an amputation. [...] Today, quite suddenly, billions of people have access to AI systems that provide augmentations, and inflict amputations, far more substantial than anything McLuhan could have imagined. This is the main thing I worry about currently as far as AI is concerned. I follow conversations among professional educators who all report the same phenomenon, which is that their students use ChatGPT for everything, and in consequence learn nothing. We may end up with at least one generation of people who are like the Eloi in H.G. Wells's The Time Machine, in that they are mental weaklings utterly dependent on technologies that they don't understand and that they could never rebuild from scratch were they to break down. Earlier I spoke somewhat derisively of lapdogs. We might ask ourselves who is really the lapdog in a world full of powerful AIs.

I think he's on the mark here, and I think his ideas about setting AIs versus AIs is thought-provoking, too. It's a good read.

(As an aside, we need to be critical of those things that make us want to use generative AI. This includes work that is stupid, work that someone convinces us needs to be done quickly, or work for the sake of work. These are all major problems.)

Edited and revised on May 19, 2025.

Squeezed

May 07, 2025

How much more expensive does working at my University and living in Lexington have to get before it's not worth it to work for this University and live in this city?

As of next year, the cost of our health insurance plan will have increased by 62.15% in the last three years. In 2022, I paid 36.4% of that amount, but as of next year, I'll pay 39.04% while my employer pays the remainder. Overall, my employer will pay 59.1% more than they did in 2022, but I'll pay 70.5% more.

Likewise, in August, my rent will have increased by 31.8% since 2022.

Meanwhile, my salary will only have increased by 8.8%. That's not nothing, but because it's nowhere near the pace of my expenses, it does mean more household cuts, on top of already making lots of cuts.

I've started selling my bread to help make up for the extra costs. I didn't want to do it, but I guess it's normal for faculty nowadays to have side hustles, and the last thing I wanted to do was more technology as a side hustle. At least making sourdough is kind of a break.

Hey, I'm grateful. I don't currently live in war zone, everyone is healthy, my kids seem happy, and I'm still able to make ends meet. But this continual squeezing has got to stop at some point.

Also, my employer decided to contract some health-AI company to help us select our benefits. Why in god's name would they do this? And, how much does it cost (and how much does that cost take away from us), and why would I want to give some random health-AI company my health information?

Did I Earn a PhD?

May 03, 2025

ChatGPT o3 is one of OpenAI's new models and has been touted as excellent at PhD-level science. I was therefore curious to see how ChatGPT would evaluate my now 12 year old PhD dissertation, and I specifically wanted to know if it would vote in favor of awarding me a PhD if it served on my dissertation committee.

I wanted to limit any bias ChatGPT has so I began the exchange with o3 with a prompt to develop criteria for evaluating a LIS PhD dissertation. In the general settings, I have also instructed it to push back on any claims I make. I want to engage with it constructively and not work with something that will engage in confirmation bias or ego building.

I therefore began the exchange with o3 by asking the following:

If you were on a dissertation committee for a LIS PhD student, what criteria would you use to evaluate the dissertation? Let's say the research uses a quantitative methodology.

o3 then created the following criteria, and each criterion included several sub-criteria. The criteria include descriptions, but I'm leaving that out for the sake of some brevity.

It's five broad buckets for evaluating a quantitative LIS PhD:

  1. Contribution & Significance (≈25%)
    • Clear problem framing
    • Originality
    • Impact potential
  2. Conceptual & Theoretical Rigor (≈20%)
    • Theoretical scaffolding
    • Construct clarity
    • Hypotheses/research questions
  3. Methodological Soundness (≈35%)
    • Design & Data
      • Sampling strategy
      • Power analysis
      • Instrumentation
    • Analysis
      • Statistical appropriateness
      • Assumption diagnostics
      • Effect size & confidence intervals
      • Robustness checks
    • Reproducibility & Transparency
      • Data/code availability
      • Ethical handling
  4. Interpretation & Discussion (≈10%)
    • Alignment of claims and evidence
    • Integration with prior work
    • Limitations
  5. Presentation & Scholarly Writing (≈10%)
    • Organization
    • APA/JASIST reporting standards
    • Plain-language summary

Based on that criteria, o3 created the following Rubric in Practice:

Bucket Unsatisfactory Satisfactory Commendable
Contribution Minor twist on old question Clear gap addressed Evident field-shifting potential
Theory Thin citation quilt Solid framework, minor leaps Elegant integration & critique
Methods Convenience sample, shaky stats Competent design, few flaws Exemplary rigor & transparency
Interpretation Over-claims or under-analysis Balanced reading of results Insightful synthesis & future agenda
Writing Disorganized, jargon-dense Clear, professional prose Engaging, publishable "as is"

o3 added the following comment about the rubric:

A dissertation can scrape by with "Satisfactory" across the board, but to earn enthusiastic signatures I want at least one "Commendable" area and no "Unsatisfactory."

It then included the following Common Deal-Breakers I Flag:

  1. P-hacking red flags: Multiple comparisons with no correction, garden-of-forking-paths storytelling.
  2. Data dredging labeled as theory testing: Exploratory is fine; just label it honestly.
  3. Opaque preprocessing: We removed outliers without criteria, or undisclosed weighting schemes.
  4. No practical significance discussion: Statistical nuance is worthless if you can't tell a librarian what to do with the result.

It offered the following final thought, which I thought was good. It's the same thing I tell PhD students, and it's the same thing my advisor told me:

I always remind candidates that the dissertation is the first draft of their career, not their magnum opus. Perfection isn't required, but methodological integrity and a meaningful contribution are non-negotiable.

As an aside, I'm struck by how it plays the actor in these exchanges. That is, I'm struck by how often ChatGPT presents itself as an actual person in an actual role. For example, it added:

And if your R scripts run on my laptop without a hitch–that earns instant goodwill.

I next asked it to use the rubric to evaluate my 2013 dissertation. I uploaded the dissertation and wrote:

This is great. Please use your rubric to honestly evaluate my PhD dissertation, which is attached. Note that this dissertation is twelve years old now, so please take that into consideration. However, once you've used your rubric to evaluate my dissertation, please state whether you would vote to award me with a PhD. I have thick skin, so be honest and critical.

I'm not going to include all the commentary, because this post would be too lengthy, but here is a table of its response. It used a three-point scale where Unsatisfactory = 1, Satisfactory = 2, and Commendable = 3.

Bucket Score
Contribution & Significance 2.5/3
Conceptual & Theoretical Rigor 2/3
Methodological Soundness 2/3
Interpretation & Discussion 2/3
Presentation & Scholarly Writing 2.5/3

In summary, it wrote an Overall verdict:

Average bucket score ≈2.2/3 ⇒ comfortably Satisfactory-plus.. Methodological transparency, a sensitive quantitative design, and a genuine empirical contribution outweigh the caveats about theoretical stretch and replicability. If I'd been on your committee in 2013 I would have voted Pass–award the PhD while asking for two post-defense revisions:

  1. Deposit the cleaned dataset + R scripts in an institutional repository for full reproducibility.
  2. Tighten the Bayes argument with a sensitivity check on the prior and an explicit discussion of alternatives (e.g., starting-point shares by field).

Twelve years on, I'd still sign–recognizing that any study of fast-moving scholarly-communication tech inevitably ages, but the dissertation met the period's standards of rigor and insight. Well done.

My Quick Thoughts

I think the rubric it created was spot on. Without knowing my dissertation's details, it created a rubric designed to evaluate all sorts of quantitative methodologies.

I think the application of the rubric was honest and appropriately critical.

I hadn't realized that I hadn't uploaded my R scripts and data to my institutional repository (or like) until it critiqued me for that. I'm happy o3 is all for open science, and annoyed with myself for not depositing my scripts and data. I would have scored higher in this category! In any case, I still have all of it, so I may upload it to GitHub.

o3 also thought I had made a theoretical stretch. I totally get that because I'm generally inclined to make these sorts of connections across disciplines. Specifically, it wrote that my dissertation ambitiously marries bounded-rationality decision theory, game theory, bibliometrics, and social-computing models.

After a follow up question, it gave me some great ideas and sources I could use to extend this line of research. The data I used for my dissertation came from CiteULike, which has shutdown. It knew that, and it offered alternative, good replacements. I also used Google Scholar as a data source, and it noted how much Google Scholar has exploded in the last decade plus. I think o3 is implying that if I pick this research up again, I might be able to develop a more rigorous theory. I still think that needs to be done, and that bounded-rationality decision theory, game theory, bibliometrics, and social-computing models all are ripe for some kind of unification in my field.

o3 also suggested ways to improve my Bayesian analysis. At the time, I used it mostly as a proof of concept. o3 accepted that as an appropriate defense, but it suggested how I could improve my priors and address the sensitivity issue.

Overall, I feel a but humbled by this, but I'm honestly happy that I apparently passed its analysis and earned a PhD in its view.

On Sense-Making

April 27, 2025

I was fortunate to meet and help Brenda Dervin with an ASIS&T workshop on sense-making back in 2012 when I was in the last year of my PhD program. She was really delightful, it was fun to work with her, and I learned a lot in that workshop.

Despite that workshop and years of reading her work on sense-making, it wasn't until this morning, while reading a random NY Times article on MAHA, that Dervin's sense-making ideas viscerally hit home for me. This is the passage from the NY Times piece that got me:

Ross Douthat: I think debates about the limitations of the U.S. medical system tend to be polarized in a very, shall we say, unhealthy way. On one side, you have people who haven’t hit the limits of medical consensus and knowledge in their own lives, and therefore struggle to understand why so many of their fellow citizens would want to wander outside those limits in search of answers or wisdom. On the other side, you have people with very good reasons for skepticism of conventional wisdom, but who have allowed that skepticism to become total, making them reject everything the establishment says while fastening onto a specific outsider narrative as an absolute alternative even if the evidence is lacking.

Teaching ed(1)

It's going smashingly because they have zero preconceptions of how an editor (or word processor) ought to be, or what those things are.

A lot of people think that the command line interface is hard. It's really not. It's mainly up against an interface (i.e., the GUI) that's {well-,too-}ingrained for them.

On gnuplot

April 08, 2025

I don't remember how I stumbled upon it, but in my early days of using Linux, I found SDF, a Public Access UNIX system. SDF runs on NetBSD, and offers, among other things, a shell account, an online bulletin board system, web hosting, and more. It's been in operation since 1987.

Sometime in the early 2000s while on SDF (where I still have an account and login periodically), I found and started using gnuplot, an interactive plotting program (see man gnuplot). This was a handful of years before I learned about or how to use R or Python. In those days, I was playing around with Perl and various shell environments, like ksh and tcsh, before finally settling on bash, and terminal-based email clients like Alpine and Elm before sticking to Mutt. Bash and Mutt are still the main programs I use today, and so those early days were pretty formative for me.

I taught myself to use gnuplot by downloading daily weather temperatures using a local university weather station, and plotting the results. If I recall correctly, I had a cron job setup that would automatically retrieve the data and update the plot and add it to a web page. It was pretty fun working on that little project.

Then I started to learn R and decades went by (geez!). But I've been wanting to dive back into gnuplot because I remember it being super powerful and fun albeit a bit cryptic. Also, unlike R, gnuplot doesn't require a graphical system to be installed. This makes it ideal for running it on remote servers with command line only environments.

Finally then, the other day I installed the terminal-based gnuplot-nox package because I wanted to use it to plot my utility usage. It took some time to relearn some things. Every online tutorial I found made too many assumptions or glossed over tacit knowledge, especially over how data files should be formatted. But ChatGippity came to the rescue, and pretty soon, I was able to plot my utility data and easily save the output as ASCII plots. This latter part was important because this is a text-only page, and if I want to share these plots, they'd have to be ASCII plots. (R does have the ability to print all sorts of ASCII plots, too, though.)

Here's a basic gnuplot script for generating an ASCII plot of my electricity usage since March 2023:

#!/usr/bin/env gnuplot

set terminal dumb size 100,30
set output 'electrical.txt'
set xdata time
set timefmt "%Y-%m"
set format x "%b\n%Y"
plot "electrical.csv" using 1:2 with lines title "Electricity Usage"
set output

The electrical.csv file is tab delimited. It looks like the following. gnuplot ignores lines starting with a pound sign #, so variable names have to be defined in the plot commands:

# Date  Usage (KWH) Avg Temp (F)
2023-05 831 58
2023-06 799 66
2023-07 942 72
2023-08 1499    78
2023-09 1175    77
2023-10 958 71
2023-11 988 62
2023-12 1024    49
2024-01 907 45
2024-02 1261    34
2024-03 1040    46
2024-04 842 50
2024-05 612 60

2024-07 570 76
2024-08 551 79
2024-09 505 77
2024-10 369 73
2024-11 276 60
2024-12 300 56
2025-01 612 41
2025-02 495 28
2025-03 531 40
2025-04 346 53

The script above only plots the first two columns: "Date" and "Usage (KWH)". I'm still working on creating more advanced plots or using statistical functions like linear regressions, but the above script produces a nice, basic usage plot over time. Here's my electricity usage since March 2023:

Alternate Day Fasting Update

April 08, 2025

Last Saturday marked three weeks of continual alternate day fasting, where I fast for 36 hours and then break the fast for 12 hours. The results have been great: I've lost 12 pounds as of Saturday.

Also, last Saturday I was eligible to donate blood again. One cool personal benefit is that they provide basic blood results. In the last four or so times I've given blood, my total cholesterol was not horrible but it was borderline high and had gone up a bit when I last donated. But yesterday my results were reported from Saturday's donation, and my total cholesterol dropped 34 points! I'm pretty psyched by that.

My next physical is in less than three months, and I hope to move my cholesterol levels down further, and see what the HDL/LDL etc breakdown is. I'll also be eligible to donate blood again before my next physical, so I'll know more sooner. I'm interested in hearing what my doctor thinks of all this. Although it's pretty subjective, I've also been feeling more alert and focused.

Surprisingly, oddly, curiously, I find that I quite look forward to each fasting period. When I first started this, it was difficult to fast for 36 hours straight, but then I felt like I got accustomed to it quickly. Now it feels like I barely notice that I'm fasting. These are all unexpected feelings.

Unexpected Outcomes

March 30, 2025

I started the alternate-day fasting a little over two weeks ago. It's been challenging at times (as expected), but I'm really liking it. It feels good for me and energizing. I find that I looking forward to fasting days about as much as I look forward to break days. It's an odd contradiction. Or perhaps not. When we're hot, we want to be cooler, and when we're cold, we want to be warmer.

But two things happened that I never expected. First, even though I eat every other day and throughout a twelve-hour period on break days, the hunger builds. Last night, at the 14 day mark, I decided to break my fast about 15 hours early and have dinner because I was ravenous. Maybe it's because I've lost a lot of weight already and my body is fighting that. I don't know, but I don't remember ever feeling that hungry before. It feels like the hunger builds over time, which is interesting and insightful. Sad, too, because it made me think about those who experience chronic hunger in famines or because of poverty.

Something else happened that was far more interesting and far more unexpected. At about 10 days in, late in the evening before the end of a fasting period, a thought came to me. It was this deep realization that I have pretty much eaten every single day of my life. And I was blown away but such a silly, obvious thought, and upon thinking it, was overwhelmed by this spiritual level of gratitude for having been able to eat 99.99% of all my days. I can't stop thinking about it, and I feel the need to reflect on it a lot more and consider how it might lead to something.

Thirty-Six Hours

March 16, 2025

This morning I completed a thirty-six hour fast. It was my second attempt this week. I made my first attempt after dinner on Thursday night, but I only lasted 24 hours and ate dinner Friday night. After Friday's dinner, I started again and was able to last until this (Sunday) morning.

The first twenty-four hours seem to be the most difficult, which is why I gave in Friday night. But by this morning, I felt like I could have kept fasting, and I felt okay at the twenty-four mark on the second attempt, too.

I've attempted other versions of intermittent fasting, like the sixteen hour fast, but I've never felt any benefit from it. This thirty-six hour fast is totally different. I was motivated to give it a try because I've always had problems with weight gain. Throughout my whole life, I've often fluctuated twenty to thirty pounds, and I've found that it doesn't get any easier to manage as I get older. But also I've been having a lot of recent problems with bloat (sorry, tmi), and I was hoping a thirty-six hour fast would help.

It seems to have helped completely with bloating. Generally, too, I started to feel really great at the twenty-hour mark, and it kept getting better. This morning, before I broke fast, I felt wonderful and alert.

My big mistake this morning was to eat too much carbs (I made a egg sandwich on a bagel). The advice I've seen is to focus more on protein when breaking the fast. But, I did feel full pretty quickly, and I still feel full even though it's been well over three hours since I ate. That's not normal for me, which is good. It's my understanding that longer satiation is another benefit.

My short-term goal for the next three to four months is to embrace alternate-day fasting (ADF). I'll fast for thirty-six hours, break fast for twelve hours, and then start the fast again. Once I reach my weight goal, which is about thirty pounds lighter than I am now, I'll try to fast for thirty-six hours once a week.

A New School

March 14, 2025

I dreamed that I recruited people to start a new library and information science school, akin to the handful of alternative / non-traditional colleges that already exist. It was independent of any institution, and inexpensive for students, but generated enough income to give its professors decent lives.

The school dismissed itself of arbitrary constructs like semesters and grading. Instead of semesters, which force subjects to be taught discretely and under arbitrary time constraints, we would interweave subject matter throughout the curriculum. Instead of grades, educators would keep journals of students that narrated their progress. Students would have access to those journals, respond to entries in them, and be able to spend as much time needed on a topic before moving on to another.

We would read, write, discuss, code, and play a lot. It would be rigorous but fun. It would inspire learning and contribute to the profession.

On Stage Fright

March 13, 2025

I started public speaking in high school when I joined the speech club, and I've been giving talks, presentations, lectures, etc fairly consistently throughout the last 18 years. Yet, I still get the nerves, and it usually presents as my voice cracking. It's the worse but also kind of funny. Every time it happens, I laugh on the inside at the absurdity of it, even though I'm panicking, too. Absurdity makes me laugh.

There's one tactic that absolutely prevents the panic and the voice cracking. If I memorize the first sentence of the presentation, the nerves go away and the voice never cracks. It works every time I use this tactic. I even use this tactic in group meetings because I get the nerves in those situations, too. Before I say something, I'll formulate and remember exactly the first thing I want to say.

The problem is that sometimes I convince myself that I don't need to do this. I'll think I've got the talk, the lecture, the slides, etc down, and I can just begin with the idea of what I want to say and not with exactly what I want to say. It never works.

I think I'm writing this post to remind me not to let me trick myself again. Sean: Always memorize the first sentence of your talk!

And yes, this is in response to a talk I gave recently.

My car is in the shop for the seventh time since I bought it at the end of last June. The fuel system is wonky. I could smell gas the other day on the way to my girlfriend's house. I turned around because the smell grew overpowering. The car stalled about two blocks from home. I was able to pull over, restart it, and make it back to my house. I popped the hood while it was still running, and gasoline was spraying all over the engine. Thankfully it didn't catch fire or worse. When it comes out of the shop, the entire fuel system will have been replaced. Hopefully I won't have any more problems with it. It'd be nice to drive a vehicle I can trust. This is the last time I buy a Chrysler vehicle.

Most scary of all: A couple of weeks ago my two younger children were rear ended while with their Mom. Fortunately, they were okay. It was a anxious phone call to receive because I woke up that morning with a disturbing premonition. When their Mom called after the accident, because of that premonition, the safety of my children was the first thing I thought of when I saw her name on caller ID. I am so happy they are well. They are my life and my ultimate loves. The car was totaled though.

I'm teaching an extra course this semester. I originally volunteered to teach a different course but was asked to switch a month or so before the end of the fall semester. I'll just say that I made a mistake accepting the switch.

Despite all of the above, I'm feeling healthy and energized. I'm teaching a course called semantic web development, and I'm writing the textbook for it as I go. Upon completion, this will be my fifth open access textbook. These textbooks are living documents that I update each time I teach the associated course.

It's been quite a bit of fun to write these textbooks. Much of my success with them has a lot to do with the process I'm using. I write sections in markdown and use mdBook to convert them into ebooks. The files for these books are stored in their own GitHub repos, and I use GitHub Pages and Actions to deploy the updates to their websites.

My good experience with writing and producing these textbooks led to a recent epiphany. A few years ago, I started writing a scholarly book on tacit knowledge and open science, but I haven't made much headway with it. Then I realized I should use the same process for this work as I do for the textbooks. I don't know why I didn't think of this before, but I'm excited to begin migrating to this new process. I think it'll be invigorating and motivating.

On Energy Use

January 17, 2025

Of course we hear a lot of talk about the environmental impact of AI, like ChatGPT, etc. And it's true that it's a lot.

But then, people don't seem to be concerned by the impact of their more mundane behaviors—and entirely too focused on what's shiny.

Consider the email.

Per ChatGPT, an AI query consumes up to ten times more energy than an email, and its usage results in about 26,280 metric tons of CO2 at an estimated 100 million or so queries per day. However, given email's dominance, with an estimated 347 billion emails per day, its usage results in about 73 million metric tons of CO2.

Takeaway: per-email impact is low, but its massive scale has massive consequences: i.e., we can estimate that email produces 2,777 times more CO2 emissions than AI queries do currently.

By the way, I asked ChatGPT to estimate the environmental impact of adding signatures to emails and of sending emails in HTML rather than in plain text. After all, sending more data over the internet consumes more resources.

ChatGPT estimates that if we assume the average email signature is 30 KB and that about half of all emails include a signature, then email signatures produce about 19 million metric tons of CO2 annually, and HTML emails (HTML coded text) contribute an estimated 4.3 million metric tons of CO2 annually.

I find it rather hilarious that email signatures and HTML code contribute about 23.3 million metric tons of CO2, and that this gets no attention. Because it's not shiny.

If we sent plain text emails and chopped off our signatures, then email would produce 1891 times more CO2 than AI queries. That's a lot but it's also a lot lower than 2,777 times more.

Perhaps we should just stop emailing?

NOTE: The data and statistics above have not been verified. But even if the numbers ChatGPT uses are liberal estimates, I would still wager that email contributes way more CO2 emissions than AI queries currently do, that its other environmental impacts are much bigger, given its scale, and that we could make better, albeit mundane, choices that would reduce our impact quite a bit.

Command Line Magic

January 03, 2025

A year ago I stumbled upon YAZ and was immediately hooked. The YAZ package includes a few utilities, such as the yaz-client command line utility and more. I can use yaz-client to establish a connection to a library's catalog, and then I can perform queries using the Prefix Query Format.

To demonstrate, below I start the yaz-client from the Bash shell, marked by the $ prompt. This creates an interactive environment to run various yaz commands, marked by the Z> prompt. The open [server-addr] command connects to a catalog's server at [server-addr]. For example, below I connect to the Library of Congress's catalog. Once the connection has been established, I use the find command to initiate a query. Then I use the show command to peruse the results, which are displayed in MARC format.

$ yaz-client
Z> open z3950.loc.gov:7090/voyager
Z> find @and @attr 1=4 "information" @attr 1=21 "library science"
Z> show 1
Z> show 2
Z> quit
$

In the example query, the find command initiates a Boolean AND search (@and) for works with "information" in the title (@attr 1=4) and the term "library science" as the subject heading (@attr 1=21). The attributes are listed in man 7 bib1-attr. The list of attributes makes it possible to create complex search commands, much more complex than what is possible via a library's discovery service.

I won't show the instructions here, but I can use the yaz-client to download retrieved sets instead of perusing records one by one. However, a set is downloaded as a single MARC file, which isn't very human readable. But the YAZ package includes a utility called yaz-marcdump. I can use that utility to convert the MARC files into JSON, and then use jq to parse the JSON file.

As an example, the MARC 650 field is for Subject added entry. It includes subfields like $a for the Topical Term, $x for General Subdivision, and $z for Geographical Subdivision. Consider a work with the following subject entry in its MARC record format:

650  0 $a Libraries $z Great Britain $v Periodicals.

This says that the work is a periodical that includes articles about Great Britain libraries.

Once data like that has been downloaded and converted into JSON, I can investigate the geographical coverage of the retrieved set using jq:

jq '.fields[] | select(has("650")) | .["650"].subfields[] | select(has("z")) | .z' records.formatted.json

And then I can map that data in R or Python! This would make a retrieved data set much more interesting to examine than what is currently offered in library search and discovery systems. The yaz-client is quite fun.

2024 Year in Review

December 31, 2024

As I mend from last week's craziness, I thought I'd write a year in review post. Alas, I have nothing meaningful to say. But in the absence of substance, I have metrics.

In summary, I wrote 122 posts this year, much more than prior years:

122 2024
 24 2023
 25 2022
 84 2021
 49 2020
 12 2019

I have no idea why I was so much more posty in 2024 than I was in 2023, but I like it.

To get those stats, I used this command:

grep "article id" index.html | awk -F"=" '{ print $2 }' | sed 's/"//' | grep --color=never -Eo "^[[:digit:]]{4}" | uniq -c

The command is a series of piped commands, where the output of one command becomes the input for the next command. The first command uses grep to search this index.html file for the unique article id string for each post. It returns lines like <article id="20190423">.

The output gets piped through awk. I set the equal sign as the field separator, and print the second column. It returns lines like "20190423">.

I want the first four numbers in that line because these represent the years of the posts. To get there, I remove the opening quote mark. I do that by piping the prior output to the sed command, which removes the first quote mark. It returns lines like 20190423">.

To get the first four digits, which represent the year for each post, I pipe the output of the prior command to grep. This returns lines like 2019.

The output is already sorted, so I pipe that to the uniq -c command, which gives me the tabular count above.

As I wrote this, I realized I didn't need awk at all. This command produces the same tabular count:

grep "article id" index.html | grep --color=never -Eo "\"[[:digit:]]{4}" | sed 's/"//' | uniq -c

There are probably more efficient ways to get the info, but still, the first command took just a minute to write. Using Linux/Unix command line utilities is so fun.

On the PageRank Algorithm

November 26, 2024

The implicit early bias in the PageRank algorithm is that it modeled itself on citation analysis and bibliometrics, and specifically on two concepts in citation analysis: bibliographic-coupling and co-citation. Bibliographic coupling occurs when two or more works reference a common work. For example, Paper A and Paper B both cite Paper C. Co-citation occurs in the other direction. It happens when two or more documents are cited together by other documents. For example, Paper C cites Paper A and Paper B. These concepts assume a theory of similarity. If Paper A and Paper B both cite Paper C (bibliographic coupling), then Papers A, B, and C share similarities or a similarity. Likewise, if Paper A and Paper B are cited by Paper C (co-citation). While vastly useful, the concepts are not granular enough to show how these papers are specifically similar. Their similarities could be based on shared theories, shared methods, shared findings, or even something more specific. Here is where other information retrieval methods, such as text analysis and tf-idf, bolster relevance in search.

In one of Page and Brin's early papers on PageRank, the authors refer to the library and information scientist Eugene Garfield, the founder of what is now the Web of Science (WOS), and then other works on citation analysis and by librarians. Garfield and many others had developed a citation analysis literature based on the assumption that citing behaviors, like described above, can represent similarity. This is helpful because similarity is an important part of relevance ranking. And then Garfield built what is now the Web of Science based on that literature and used WOS to test this and related theories. Page and Brin, I suppose being academics first, were influenced by this literature and the Web of Science database, which they surely used in school.

In their paper, Page and Brin highlight the concepts of backlinks (inedges) and forward links (outegdges). Although not perfectly aligned, these concepts correspond to bibliographic-coupling and co-citation. Backlinks occur when a web page is linked to by others, and forward links are the links in a web page to other web pages. Backlinks most resemble bibliographic coupling because multiple sources (pages) point to a common target. For example, if web page A and web page B both link to web page C, that's analogous to bibliographic coupling. Thus, backlinks represent a type of relevance of influence: the more backlinks (the more pages that link to a specific page), the more influential that page.

These concepts work well for academic literature, because in comparison to the wild web, we can lightly assume a homogeneity in quality, given the peer review process. However, this assumption has faults because there are all sorts of biases in peer review, and these biases are only exacerbated on the web, which doesn't have any gatekeeping.

Although Page and Brin are concerned about the quality of web content, which they discuss in their paper, they argue that "backlinks...provide a kind of peer review." This is like saying that bibliographic coupling provides quality control. And this assumption gets promoted when accounting for frequency; i.e., it assumes that more backlinks correlate to more quality control. Even if that works for academic literature, it's a big leap to make the assumption that it works for web content. PageRank cannot measure or account for quality.

The problem with the PageRank algorithm is that it assumes people who publish on the web are, on average, information literate: that they know when information is needed, how to access and evaluate that information through critical thinking and analysis, and how to incorporate that information into new information. It's the information science version of economic's homo economicus but instead a kind of homo informatio. This is an okay if strong assumption to make when it comes to academic literature (researchers cite for a whole host of reasons and these reasons are often biased, too), but it turned out to be a dangerous assumption for ranking web content, where linking is much lazier, at best, and nefarious, at worst. For example, the PageRank algorithm assumes that people link based solely on content and not based on generating sales or ad revenue. In the end, there is much more self-interest involved in linking than in citing, although self-interest has long been a problem in citing behavior, too.

The problem with both citing and linking, as measured above, is not that they assume similarity, it's that they also assume influence (bibliographic coupling/backlinking plus frequency) is a proxy for quality. It's a big leap because neither is even a perfect measure of influence, either.

Pancake Resilience

November 22, 2024

I make pancakes often. I whisk 1.5c flour, 2t baking powder, 1t baking soda, and a pinch of salt, or 1/4t. Then I stir in 1.5c milk, 2 eggs, and 2T of oil. These ingredients form the batter.

Sometimes I don't have all of the ingredients. When a thing like that happens, I adjust. I've subbed water for milk, extra oil for a missing egg, melted butter for oil, or whatever.

Each time, the batter turns out alright. The pancakes are good. They're pancakes regardless of the adjustment. How far can I take this before they're not? Pancakes are so resilient. And why do I think about this so much!

There's a serious problem with what people know in this country, and our lack of education and ability to learn hurts us badly.

Harder to Accommodate than to Assimilate

November 11, 2024

Let me borrow from Jean Piaget's theory of cognitive development and apply it at the macro level. It's probably not valid but so be it. I'm thinking about collective cognitive development or collective learning, folks!

Here's what I'm brainstorming...this is a very rough sketch and maybe a bit harebrained.

What if we apply Piaget's theory to society rather than to the individual? Let's define society as a people that share some substantial mental frameworks or models or schemas of the world and how the world works. More pointedly, let's think of society as a person. Per Piaget, when new information is presented to a person, the person either integrates the new information into an existing schema, or the person modifies an existing schema or creates a new schema to accommodate the new information.

Let's take it as a given that the world is changing quite rapidly and that this entails confrontation with constant new information. Well then, society-as-person has two options: assimilate or accommodate. By far, the more difficult of the two is accommodation. Accommodation is an uncomfortable process because it challenges our worldviews. To accommodate new information, our worldviews or our schemas of the world must change. Or we create new schemas. Assimilating new information is simpler and easier because it requires inserting new information into existing, immutable schemas.

Piaget's theory originally aimed at children, who are much more flexible and open to new information. But adults are not often so adaptable. And so adults struggle to accommodate. They get stuck in a mode of assimilation and try to fit the complexities of a changing world into an unchanging, rigid schema. It's safer for them this way. It's safer to maintain their existing narratives or schemas and contort new information, deny it, or adopt conspiratorial thinking so that their schemas remain stable.

Assimilating new information is not bad, per se. Per Piaget, equilibrium between assimilation and accommodation is needed, otherwise we would neither learn new concepts (constant assimilation) nor would we maintain a stable worldview (constant accommodation).

Under this view, we might ask: what if human development, at the societal level, is at some disequilibrium between assimilation and accommodation? What if in fact there is a battle between these two processes? What if the make-you-know-what-great-again represents an assimilating move? There are all sorts of fears that would motivate half of the country to make that move.

I don't know. This might be harebrained, but it's helping me to think about last week in a more empathetic and non-cynical light. So perhaps I'll continue to think through it for a bit and make the thesis more substantial and sophisticated.

In Praise of the Wealthy

November 07, 2024

America has achieved new heights in praise of the ultra-wealthy.

The cultural narrative has fully shifted. Wealth signals wisdom and virtue. Deeds and words are not signals of wisdom and virtue. Only wealth is. The wealthy are prophets. Their amassed wealth is evidence of their prophetic visions and their moral triumphs. This is the only evidence needed. Wealth is a yardstick for wisdom and character.

The wealthy have superior epistemological claims. They know what we do not know. They make better decisions than the non-wealthy. Their expertise is unlimited.

The wealthy possess greater credibility than the non-wealthy. They contribute more than the non-wealthy. They work harder than the non-wealthy.

All hail the Wealthy among us.

Beet Kvass

October 30, 2024

I love beets. If I was given a choice for a final meal, I'd eat boiled or roasted beets, with buttery rice doused in Worcestershire, and sauteed beet greens.

My children like beets, too, and every week I make beet burgers. The burgers are made with ground boiled beets, ground oatmeal, and cooked and then ground, dried black beans. They're pretty delicious. My oldest generally eats three of these burgers. My youngest resisted them for the longest time, but then discovered that they liked them with potato chips layered on the burger. I suppose they needed a bit of texture!

Recently I learned about beet kvass, which is a fermented drink made from fermenting beets in salt water. I ferment other things, and thought we'd give this a try. I brought a few bunches of beets home, cubed them up, added them to several mason jars, and then covered them with salt water. They've been fermenting for about seven days now. We tasted it a couple of days ago, but unfortunately it tasted like salted beet water. Hopefully it works out because by all accounts, beet kvass is awesome.

Overloaded

October 26, 2024

The more I talk with people about their use of LLMs, the more I think it's used because people are overloaded with a lot of small work. They use it so that they have more time with more meaningful work but will also use it for meaningful work when they still feel strapped for time. Or when there are other pressures on them.

Some folks decry the use of LLMs as well as the environmental damage. But I think the real problems are because we're overloaded and under various pressures to keep producing even though small work keeps rising. This all makes me think that LLMs are mostly a band aid on more systemic issues. We're a band aid culture.

In the end, even though I don't subscribe to it, I get the appeal of libertarianism. There's just so much bureaucracy. I get why that there's so much bureaucracy, too. It's because there's been too much abuse. We did this too ourselves, but the end result is that we live in a world where trust isn't or can't be the default.

Dead Hangs

Despite all this progress, I have had the hardest time dead hanging by one arm. I realized I hadn't tried it at all when my middle child started hanging by one arm. They could just do it for forever, and it looked super fun and natural. When I first tried it, though, I couldn't last a second.

So I started working on it. Then yesterday, I was by a tree talking with a friend, and I gave it another shot. And it worked! I hung by one arm for a good twenty seconds or so. Self Deprecation

September 12, 2024

Self deprecation is a great way to stay humble, and it's also a way to stay accessible to people. I realized last year, though, that toxic people don't understand self-deprecation, including self-deprecating humor. Toxic people instead interpret self-deprecation literally. When people make self-deprecations, either out of humility or humor, they interpret it as confessions of ineptitude. Consequently, they think the person is weak.

This has an unfortunate effect on many things, from faculty meetings to politics. It suppresses expressions of humility by the humble. And we need humility more than ever.

Humility is neither a weakness nor does it signal ineptitude. It's a virtue.

Being Pedantic

September 03, 2024

I read scientific literature for facts, but I read novels for truth. Right now I'm reading Donna Tartt's novel The Secret History. It's engrossing.

The main characters are college students at a small liberal arts college in the northeast. There's this one line that struck me deeply:

the young scholar is much more a pedant than his older counterpart" (p. 84).

Pedant can have a pejorative meaning, but it can also just refer to someone who is overly focused on rules and minor details in academic learning. I think that's often just part of the journey of an academic or a scholar. It certainly was part of mine. I think it's an important part of the journey, too, but I'm glad I'm past it.

My turning point to my own older counterpart came early on, when I read Susan Haack's Defending science within reason: Between scientism and cynicism. In this work, Haack quotes the physicist P.W. Bridgman, who described the scientific method as,

nothing more than doing one's damnedest [...], no holds barred (p. 24).

I've been reflecting on that line for well over a decade now. To me it means that there are multiple methods to get the facts and that being pedantic doesn't necessarily entail being rigorous.

It's complicated

August 30, 2024

As faculty, we have to report whether a student attended, engaged, or participated in a course between certain dates near the start of each semester.

We were recently informed of a new reporting requirement. Now, we also have to report whether a student attended, engaged, or participated in a course if they withdrew from the course before a specified date.

I thought it was kind of funny that I had to read the new requirement a couple of times to make sense of it. I didn't want to spend any more time on this, though, so for the lolz, I asked ChatGPT to represent the logic of these reporting requirements in Python code.

For your reading pleasure:

#!/usr/bin/env python3

from datetime import datetime

# Define the reporting period
reporting_start_date = datetime(2024, 9, 10)
reporting_end_date = datetime(2024, 9, 16)
drop_deadline = datetime(2024, 9, 15)

# Simulated student data
students = [
    {"name": "Alice", "drop_date": datetime(2024, 9, 12), "attendance_dates": [datetime(2024, 9, 11)]},
    {"name": "Bob", "drop_date": datetime(2024, 9, 14), "attendance_dates": [datetime(2024, 9, 13), datetime(2024, 9, 14)]},
    {"name": "Charlie", "drop_date": datetime(2024, 9, 10), "attendance_dates": []},
    {"name": "Diana", "drop_date": None, "attendance_dates": [datetime(2024, 9, 12), datetime(2024, 9, 15)]}
]

def check_attendance(student):
    """Check if a student attended on or before their drop date."""
    if student["drop_date"] is None:
        return None  # Student didn't drop
    for date in student["attendance_dates"]:
        if date <= student["drop_date"]:
            return "YES"
    return "NO"

def report_attendance(students, reporting_start_date, reporting_end_date, drop_deadline):
    """Report attendance for all students."""
    for student in students:
        drop_date = student["drop_date"]
        if drop_date is not None and drop_date <= drop_deadline:
            attendance_status = check_attendance(student)
            print(f"Student: {student['name']}, Drop Date: {drop_date.strftime('%Y-%m-%d')}, Attended: {attendance_status}")
        else:
            print(f"Student: {student['name']}, Drop Date: Not Dropped or after deadline, Attended: --")

# Run the reporting logic
report_attendance(students, reporting_start_date, reporting_end_date, drop_deadline)

August 28, 2024

Basic tables are fine for many things, but sometimes tables need (multi-column) headers, footers, titles, and notes and not only basic rows and columns. These things are important because tables are an important visual organization of data.

The need for more functionality has long spurred me to consider using Asciidoc more. I've only played around with it and perused its docs, but I like what it has to offer. It is mostly as straightforward as Markdown (but not quite).

My current annoyance with Markdown started yesterday with my Just Chilling post below. I didn't like the table I created, which I wrote in markdown originally, and so I restructured it in HTML. But it's no fun writing in HTML, especially HTML tables. I think Asciidoc might be a better way forward.

A simple Python function to calculate percentage change:

def perchange(x,y):
    c = ((x - y) / y) * 100
    return c

RIP Josh

August 07, 2024

Over two years ago, soon after we left Texas, some of my extended family there were swimming in a pool. My Mom said to my cousin, who had put us up while we were in Texas, that he looked yellow. She had had her own liver problems and thought something might be wrong with his.

Later, he received a diagnosis of bile duct cancer. His Mom was there and helped him and his family through the initial process. A few months later, she died from cardiac arrest. I last saw my cousin at her memorial.

Josh put up a hell of a fight. He had two young children, both of which were his delight and his Mother's delight.

I last spoke with my cousin eleven days ago. The last several months were hard, and I'm not clear on what happened, but something caused some brain damage. My Mom put me on speaker phone, I called out to him, and he called back to me, repeating my name. Then I told him I loved him, and he told me he loved me, over and over again.

My cousin and I grew up with each other, and he was more like a little brother. We spent most of our summers with our common grandparents, and then my brother and I would often stay with him and my aunt and uncle.

Josh sometimes aggravated the hell out of me. He wanted things, fiercely, and that kind of desire worked well for him in life. My aggravation with him went away when I realized that I didn't want things like he did.

But even during those times, he was also someone that I wanted to live near when we would be old people. I don't think that would have happened, but I'm sorry that it's not even possible now. Josh, I'm sorry you didn't get the one thing you most wanted, to grow old with your husband and raise those two dear children of yours.

My little cousin, I have so many fond memories of us growing up, hanging with Jo and Papa, and spending our summers at the pool. You were so generous and obnoxious and a source of tremendous fun and gusto. I will miss you quite dearly. The world already feels less complete without you in it.
