Vim as File Manager

August 05, 2024

I use vim all the time. However, I have a tendency to close the application when I'm done with a particular file. I don't think this is very efficient.

A better use of vim would be to keep it open and use more of its potential. The first step is to start using it as a file manager with netrw.

One of the most basic file management actions is to move or copy a file. It took me a moment to understand the process. To move or copy a file:

  1. Open the current directory: vim . Or if already in vim, use <Esc>:e . to open the current directory.
  2. Navigate to a target directory and mark it: mt
  3. Navigate to source file and mark it: mf.
  4. Press mc or mm to copy / remove (respectively) the source file to the target directory.

I've been using vim for twenty-something years, and I'm still learning new things about it.

Breaking Bad Habits

August 01, 2024

To help me break my bad habit of checking email first thing in the morning, I wrote a Python script to limit my email usage to one hour a day, from noon to 1pm.

#!/usr/bin/env python3

import time
import subprocess
from colorama import Fore

start_hour = 12
end_hour = 13
hour = int(time.strftime("%H"))
minutes = int(time.strftime("%M"))
minutes_left = 60 - minutes

def get_email():
    mbsync = "/usr/bin/mbsync"
    mbsync_arg = "-a"
    subprocess.call([mbsync, mbsync_arg])

def process_email():
    notmuch = "/usr/bin/notmuch"
    notmuch_arg = "new"
    subprocess.call([notmuch, notmuch_arg])

def email():
    mutt = "/usr/bin/mutt"
    subprocess.call([mutt])

def check_email():
    if hour == start_hour:
        get_email()
        process_email()
        email()
    elif start_hour - hour == 1:
        print(Fore.YELLOW + "Almost there. %s more minutes to check email." % minutes_left)
    elif hour < start_hour:
        hour_diff = (start_hour - hour) - 1
        print(Fore.RED + "BE PATIENT. %s more hour(s) and %s minutes to check email." % (hour_diff, minutes_left))
    elif (hour + 1) > end_hour:
        print(Fore.GREEN + "WAIT TILL TOMORROW TO CHECK EMAIL.")

def main():
    check_email()

main()

It's a pretty simple script and is mainly just enough to remind me to not check my email first thing in the morning. And it only deters me from using mutt, my email client. If I keep the client open, then I can check email anytime. I could also get around it by calling mutt using the full path to the executable: /usr/bin/mutt.

But, it's been enough so far to keep me focused, which is what I want.

I call this Python script in my .bash_functions file:

mutt() {
    $HOME/bin/mutt.py
}

Reading for Fulfillment

Process Substitution

July 08, 2024

I use the pipe | operator all the time to "pass" the output of one command to the input of another command. For example, one time I needed to check which directories the SHELL used for executables. To do that, I can check my $PATH variable. However, the output is one long line of directories, where each directory is separated by a colon. This is not a very readable output.

To make the output readable, I pipe it to the sed command to replace colons with newlines:

echo "$PATH" | sed 's/:/\n/g'

We can keep adding pipes to the output. If I want to pipe the above output to list executable directories only in my home directory, I simply add the $HOME variable to the grep (aka, search) command:

echo "$PATH" | sed 's/:/\n/g' | grep "$HOME"

In the above cases, piping is a way to filter the data.

In other cases where I need to filter multiple data at the same time, I can use process substitution.

Here is an example where I use sed to filter out specific lines in multiple files, specifically, lines 7-10 in file1 and lines 1-3 in file2. The cat command joins the output together:

cat <(sed -n '7,10p' file1) <(sed -n '1,3p' file2)

If I want to save that output to another file, then I can redirect it to another file using the redirect > operator:

cat <(sed -n '7,10p' file1) <(sed -n '1,3p' file2) > file3

It's stuff like this that makes working on the command line so efficient, especially when working with data.

Searching for R

July 05, 2024

My first years learning R started in the R Console. This was back in 2008, two to three years before RStudio launched. I remember installing R for the first time and then having a hell of time launching the R Console before I realized that I had to type capital R and not simply r in the terminal. I laughed hard when I caught my error. It's one of the annoying things about the otherwise great package management system in Debian-based Linux systems: the names of packages (e.g., r-recommended on Debian-based distros) are not the same as the executables.

I also remember being confused by the R package install process. For example, I wasn't sure if I should start R as an admin (e.g., sudo R) or as a regular user (e.g., R) before running the update.packages() command. And then I was confused by which download mirror to select. Eventually I figured out how things worked.

When RStudio came out, I started using that for most projects. Even though it's a fine application, has nice functions, and its editor has Vim keybindings, I much prefer the command line and actual Vim. Thus, I've barely used it in recent years and haven't installed it in a while on my systems. My workflow has long been command line centric, but I've been making an effort over the last several years to double down on a command line workflow.

The problem is that nowadays it seems like every web search for how to do something in R turns up how to do something in RStudio. For example, I like knitr for processing documents written in Markdown and R code. This approach to producing reports aligns with Donald Knuth's idea of literate programming, which I've long found interesting. It's completely fine to knit a document directly in Vim. However, a default search for how to use knitr or other packages means you get a tutorial on using it in RStudio. In fact, most web searches these days on anything R related yields results on using tidyverse content in RStudio. It's kind of annoying, and it illustrates how search is largely a function of content and popularity.

On Soup

July 04, 2024

Soup is my favorite food. If all I could eat for the rest of my life was soup, I could live with that. I worked as a cook for about 18 years. I never seriously wanted my own restaurant, but when I imagined opening one, I imagined opening a soup and sandwich shop.

I don't often buy canned soup because soup is the easiest thing to make in the world. This is what I do for the most basic soup:

  1. Add broth to pot (or bouillon to water).
  2. Add vegetables (whatever I have on hand).
  3. Bring to boil.
  4. Add noodles.
  5. Lower the boil.
  6. Salt to taste.

And that's all there is to it.

Making creamy soups is not much more involved. Blend and add cream.

I like unblended soups, but that's easy, also. Just blend it.

In my fridge, I had a small piece of onion, some carrots, some celery, some tomato, and some cooked beets. I added it to some broth in a pot. Brought it to a boil. Added some spaghetti, which I broke up into pieces as I dropped it into the broth. Then let it low boil for about ten minutes. So good.

Custom YAML

June 05, 2024

I'm editing a manuscript and marking it up with markdown. This is not an Rmarkdown file. It's just plain markdown. I have a YAML in the header with metadata like this:

title: "This is the title"
subtitle: "This is the subtitle"
author:
    - name: "C. Sean Burns"
      affiliation: "University of Kentucky"
    - name: "Generic Name"
      affiliation: "University of Generica"
date: "June 5, 2024"
abstract:
    This is the abstract of the manuscript. It goes here.
lang: "en"

I was having a problem generating HTML from this file using pandoc. The metadata would render to HTML except for the author names and affiliations, which would simply return true:

pandoc file.md -s -o file.html

The solution was to create and use a custom template for the YAML. I had read about this before but hadn't investigated it enough. But now I have! Here's my custom template for the above example file:

<!DOCTYPE html>
<html lang="${lang}">
<head>
  <meta charset="UTF-8">
  <title>${title}</title>
</head>
<body>
  <h1>${title}</h1>
  <h2>${subtitle}</h2>
  <p><strong>Author(s):</strong></p>
  <ul>
  $for(author)$
    <li>${author.name} (${author.affiliation})</li>
  $endfor$
  </ul>
  <p><strong>Date:</strong> ${date}</p>
  <p><strong>Abstract:</strong> ${abstract}</p>
  <hr>
  $body$
</body>
</html>

It's pretty simple. For every field in the YAML that I want in the HTML output, simply enclose the field name in the custom template file. In the case of multiple fields with the same field name, such as with the author and affiliation fields, then use a for loop in the custom template file. And note that the field names are hierarchical, such that affiliation is a child of author and should thus be ${author.affiliation}, and so forth.

To create the HTML output with pandoc, do:

pandoc file.md -s -o file.html --template=custom_template.html

Of course, the file names could be descriptive of the project. And the custom template could do more with the metadata, like using the YAML metadata to add HTML <meta> tags in the HTML <head>.

Accidental Redirect

June 04, 2024

I was editing an HTML file and wanted to list the <h2> tags in the file with the grep command.

And I made a big MISTAKE because I typed too fast and pressed Enter too fast:

grep "<h2"> index.html

Astute observers will notice that the > tag is outside the right quote mark. Which means I accidentally zeroed out the file because that's a bash redirect! I had done a lot of work on that file, too. Oh, the pain!

The correct grep command is:

grep "<h2>" index.html

But next time I'll just do:

grep "h2" index.html

Fortunately I had the HTML file open in my browser. But when I right-clicked and selected View Page Source, I got nothing. Because I emptied the file!

What did the trick was pressing Ctrl-a to select all in the browser window and selecting View Selection Source. That opened up the HTML code, and I was able to paste that into my editor.

I'm using git on this file, but I hadn't yet committed my changes. Argh!!

Make Install

June 03, 2024

On Debian-based distros, including Debian but also Ubuntu and other derivatives, the common way to install software is through the apt command. And there are corresponding package management systems on other Linux families, like dnf on Red Hat-based systems. These are great software management systems, but using them generally means installing older versions of software (albeit, patched for security updates). That's usually fine, but in some cases, I would like the latest versions of software. For example, Vim 9 was released almost two years ago and is much better than the last stable release, which was Vim 8.2. But Vim 8.2 is the default version on my Linux box right now when installing using apt. Furthermore, there are some plugins that I use a lot that expect Vim 9. Some of those plugins also expect Python3 support, and the apt version of Vim isn't configured for that. These factors mean that the best way to install Vim is by downloading and compiling the source code.

Installing Vim 9

The steps to install Vim 9 (after perhaps removing Vim 8.2 using apt) are:

  1. Install dependencies needed to compile Vim 9 with Python3 support. I already had build-essentials installed but for reproducibility on a new system:
sudo apt update
sudo apt install -y build-essentials python3-dev
  1. I like to compile software from my ~/bin directory. So, change to my ~/bin directory and clone the Vim repository from GitHub:
cd ~/bin
git clone https://github.com/vim/vim.git
  1. Navigate to the new Vim source directory:
cd vim/src
  1. Configure with Python3 support:
./configure --with-features=huge --enable-python3interp --prefix=/usr/local
  1. Compile and install Vim:
make
sudo make install
  1. Then check the version using:
`vim --version | grep -A1 "^VIM"`

Updating Vim 9

The process is fairly similar when updating Vim, but there are a few differences. First, I don't need to clone the repo, but I do need to pull it for updates. I also need to run make distclean to cleanup prior build files. The whole process is:

cd ~/bin/vim
make distclean
git pull origin master
cd src/
./configure --with-features=huge --enable-python3interp --prefix=/usr/local
make
sudo make install

And then I can check the installed version number.

On Distance

June 03, 2024

My friend heard the thunderclap before I did. We were on the phone, and they live a little less than three miles away.

I wish I had the presence of mind to count how many seconds passed before I heard the thunder, but I think it was only two to three seconds.

Given the speed of sound at 74 degrees Fahrenheit, it would have taken about 13+ seconds for the sound to travel to my ears. But that's assuming the sound came from a direction directly beyond them.

However, if I am correct that the time it took the sound to reach me after them was only a couple of seconds, then that means the thunder originated between us but closer to them than to me. More specifically, it was a bit less than half mile closer to them.

Say Less

May 26, 2024

Back in 1998, I had an office job. My boss instructed me to write a response to a client's letter, and I wrote a two-page, single-spaced reply. When I showed it to him, he laughed and told me to edit it down to a couple of sentences.

I learned the same lesson years later in a graduate philosophy course. In both cases, the main point was: the more you say, the more you accept responsibility. That kind of responsibility might mean greater commitment to do something or more admittance of fault.

Twenty-six years later, I am still having to learn this lesson.

It's hard because sometimes I want to write a whole lot about something, to get it out of my system or to think through the issue. But the key for me is: once I've done that and made my point, edit out all but the point that I want to make.

Rmarkdown

May 21, 2024

I use Vim as my primary environment for R development. This setup uses the following Vim plugins:

  • Nvim-R: Vim as an R development environment
  • ncm-R: R autocompletion in Vim
  • vim-pandoc: pandoc integration
  • zotcite: Zotero integration in Vim

With the Nvim-R plugin, I can run R commands directly within the document. But the plugin shines when writing Rmarkdown (Rmd) files. To produce a report written from an Rmarkdown file in Vim, I use the following command: :Rmarkdown. This command generates the configured output as specified in the Rmarkdown YAML header. (It also assumes that the rmarkdown package is installed in R and pandoc on your system.)

I successfully used this process for a paper recently accepted by Scientometrics.

Another efficient way to create reports from Rmd files is by using a Makefile. I've been using Makefiles to generate my textbooks using mdbook, and I thought I'd apply it to this workflow. This approach allows you to share your research workflow as you intended it, and that's better for reproducibility than just sharing your code and data.

As an example, let's say I have a file named test.Rmd with the following YAML (imagine some R code and text after the YAML):

---
title: "Rmarkdown from the command line"
author: "C. Sean Burns"
date: "May 20, 2024"
output:
  html_document:
    theme: united
    keep_md: false
    df_print: paged
    code_folding: hide
    toc: true
    toc_depth: 2
  word_document: default
  pdf_document: default
always_allow_html: yes
---

[insert text and R code here ...]

With the following Makefile, I can auto generate PDF, HTML, and DOCX files:

all: test.html test.docx test.pdf

test.html: test.Rmd
    Rscript -e "rmarkdown::render('test.Rmd', output_format = 'html_document')"

test.docx: test.Rmd
    Rscript -e "rmarkdown::render('test.Rmd', output_format = 'word_document')"

test.pdf: test.Rmd
    Rscript -e "rmarkdown::render('test.Rmd', output_format = 'pdf_document')"

To generate all three files, run make all in the shell. Or to generate only the HTML file, run make test.html.

Seasonal Habits

May 14, 2024

I use the mutt email client running in a tmux window. It's an efficient and fast way to manage my email workflow. And that's really important because sometimes a semester can be particularly email heavy. The less time I'm in email, the more time I'm doing something more rewarding and productive.

Now that we're about to start our summers, I don't need to check email that frequently. But as is normal when switching seasons, I'm having a hard time breaking that habit. The mental switch will happen, but it sometimes takes a few weeks.

It's important to break the email checking habit because email interrupts focus. Summers are for reading, writing, and researching, and I need focus for those activities.

Changing a habit often takes simple measures. To curb my email checking habit more quickly, I closed that tmux window for the summer.

Sentence Line Breaks

May 14, 2024

Over the years, I have found that writing in a text editor frees me up to explore ways of writing. For example, a couple of years ago I discovered semantic line breaks. It's an old convention that originated in the early days of computing. The process involves adding line breaks to sentences after clauses, conjunctions, and other natural breaks in sentences. It works when writing in a text editor because I write using Markdown. When the file is converted to HTML or some other format, the sentences will render as natural paragraphs. I like using semantic line breaks because it makes text editing and version control easier.

However, lately I find that I prefer writing one sentence per line. It still makes text editing and version control easier, but it has other benefits. It discourages me from adding too many clauses and conjunctions, which I tend to do. Consequently, it encourages me to write shorter sentences or sentences with less breaks. I find this funny because I tend to skip over parenthetical clauses when reading but I tend to use them a lot in my writing. So it feels good to write in a way that aligns with how I prefer to read text.

Archiving Email

May 13, 2024

I archive most of my work email in maildir format, and use notmuch to index and search it. Sometimes I use these tools to look for patterns in my email. At the most basic, I sometimes wonder how much email I've archived.

So far, here's the breakdown. The current year is obviously not over with, but since 2013 when I started my current job, I've archived the following number of emails:

Email counts per year
Year Emails
2013 2319
2014 6286
2015 7513
2016 5682
2017 4383
2018 4604
2019 3817
2020 5721
2021 3425
2022 4805
2023 4924
2024 2762
Total: 56232

I archive based on the calendar year and not the academic year. Therefore 2013 was only a partial year because I started in August 2013. The spike for the years 2014 and 2015 were due to being on a big search committee. I thought at the time that the pandemic slowed email down (2021), but I was on sabbatical during the spring 2021 semester, and I'm sure that explains the big dip. Alternate forms of communication via Teams, Zoom, etc have increased since I started but not enough it seems to put a major dent in the overall average. I used to but for a long time haven't archived email from the learning management system (Blackboard in the early years and then Canvas to today). I could probably still clear some of that out of my archives. Not counting 2013 (half-year) and 2024 (not over with), I've archived an average of 20.6 emails per day in the email heaviest year (2015) and an average of 9.4 emails per day in the email lightest year (2021).

Five Years

Lost in Code

April 21, 2024

One of the challenges with being a professor is that your time (or my time) is split among different kinds of jobs: consider the standard teaching, research, and service division of labor. Even within those respective areas, there are specific kinds of work that require different skill sets: writing, analyzing data, lecturing, creating classroom excersises, grading, etc etc.

So, it seems, particularly during the school year, that I don't get the chance to spend a substantial amount of time on any one task that requires a lot of skill. That is, it often feels like I'm jumping around from one thing to another. It's one of my least favorite aspects of the job.

But today I got to spend a substantial amount of time writing code. It seems like it's been forever since the last time I was able to do that.

And I forgot how glorious it feels. The time has whooshed by.

On Scat

April 15, 2024

I have this weird ability to recall when and where I learned things.

The word scat came up recently in a conversation. I immediately recalled learning that word in 1989 in Larry McMurtry's novel, Moving On: A Novel. I checked Google Books, and the word is indeed in that book. Memory verified!

I have a fairly decent book collection. Sometimes I think I'd like to rid myself of the collection. It's just a lot of stuff. But, it's also how I index many of my memories. I can look at one of my books and say exactly when and where I was when I read it. I wonder if I rid myself of my collection, I'd rid myself of those memories. So, for now, I keep them.

As an aside, it's this connection with physical books that makes me wonder if I should stop reading ebooks and stick with pbooks. In the meantime, until I decide on that course, I've found that keeping a list of books that I've read has worked just as well.

Reading does so much good, either way.

See Also

April 13, 2024

For the most part, I think peer review is important. In my experience, it's been helpful in making sure that I write clearly. However, sometimes reviewers can make trivial comments. One reviewer was adamant that I clean up an in-text citation. I had placed the in-text citation before the end of the first part of a compound sentence. It looked used something like this:

something or other (Name1, Year), but other or something (Name2, Year).

And they wanted me to rewrite it like:

something or other, but other or something (Name1, Year; Name2, Year).

I thought that suggestion obscured the relationships between the parts of the sentence and their connections the in-text citations. To me, the in-text citation should be attached to the claim in the sentence and not just tacked on to the end.

Their motivation, I believe, was to adhere to the journal's style guide. Rules, as style guides, are useful of course, but they're useful because they provide consistency and help to clarify meaning. When rules are followed for their own sake, then the point is lost, in more ways than one.

I think we should just get rid of in-text citations anyway. We should be hyperlinking text as much as possible. It's more specific. And, I probably think that because writing for the web has conditioned me to do so. That is, hyperlinking text is more common, in everyday practice, than using in-text citations.

Funk

Copying

March 19, 2024

Sometimes I need to copy a few lines from a file but don't want to open the file because, well, I don't know why. I'd rather just stay on the command prompt.

Since I'm using Wayland nowadays, I use wl-copy to copy to the clipboard. To copy some lines from a text file, just start with sed and pipe to wl-copy:

sed -n '/regex/p' file.md | wl-copy

Reload

March 16, 2024

There's an annoying browser behavior where if you return to a tab after moving away from it for a while, upon reopening the tab, the entire web page will reload, and so you lose your place on the page.

There are all sorts of reasons why this happens, but the fundamental cause is the use of JavaScript and JS Frameworks. JavaScript is simply not necessary on the vast majority of websites even though it's used on the vast majority of websites. HTML and CSS are great, powerful, low bandwidth, and very fast.

Ditch JavaScript and its frameworks 99% of the time.

R Base

March 13, 2024

I met a civil engineer recently. She uses R for her job, but here's the catch: her and her colleagues are restricted to using the R Base Package, without the allowance for any additional libraries or packages. I find this fascinating and can't wait to ask her more questions.

I also find this setup inspiring. While the tidyverse and similar alterations for other languages offer some conveniences and a more Python like syntax, my first experiences with R predate their existence (outside of ggplot, which was available then). The problem with the R alterations is that they are unstable, and the endless deprecation warnings I get when using them are disconcerting. I'd wager that I could still run my scripts from my early days with R without issue, but I wouldn't wager any such thing for scripts that I wrote just a year or two ago that use those add-ons. (See also: dependency hell.)

The enduring reliability of the Base R package highlights the challenge, for open science and more, with relying on add-ons. I think this is true for much of the software ecosystem. I'm getting a sense that more people are pushing back against software frameworks that contort the basic languages they build upon, like React with JavaScript. We'll have to wait to see if that push back gains momentum.

What's interesting to me is that programming libraries and frameworks have long existed and were created to help programmers develop applications for specific contexts. For example, ncurses helps C programmers develop command line applications. But the difference here is that C is a general purpose programming language; ncurses provides a context or domain for C programming (the command line environment). Programming languages like R and JavaScript are more single-minded languages. That is, they were built specifically for statistics and web development, respectively. The add-ons do not change that context or domain. Contextual enhancement is fine and is not the same thing as fundamental alteration.

I think the fundamentals of languages are way more important to learn than the alteration frameworks. Back in the 90s, I remember watching an interview with Michael Jordan. He was asked about how he practices, and whether he spends most of his time on jump shots. His response was that he spent most of his practice on the fundamentals: passing, dribbling, shooting, etc., and he seemed to strongly believe that it was this focus on the fundamentals, along with practicing them a lot, that helped him excel. I've seen other great players make similar claims, like Kobe Bryant.

There might be some utility in learning frameworks that alter programming languages that were already created for specific purposes, but in the end, I will always argue that it is so much more important to develop a deep understanding of the fundamental aspects of a programming language and that doing so is crucial for excellence in coding and for maintaining a healthy and robust software ecosystem. Add-ons might be useful for specific tasks, like pandas for Python for contextual enhancement, but not replacements for the fundamental languages themselves: not for open science, for learning, or for a healthy software ecosystem.

Bashing Bash

March 12, 2024

There are legitimate criticisms against writing complex programs in Bash, but when it comes to working with text in files, using bash with other languages like sed and awk seems to me unrivaled.

Background: I spent some time rewriting the code I use to automate posting to this page. I thought I'd rewrite the code in Python, and while I have most of it working, it is so much more verbose and unwieldy than its bash counterpart.

Plus, languages like bash, sed, and awk are much more stable than Python and its various dependencies. I already can't count how many times I've been burned by a dependency update that breaks code in Python or R.

Water Temp

March 07, 2024

I take cold showers every day. I don't think I've missed a day in two years, and I have only missed two days in two in a half years.

This experience has allowed for neat observations. As an example, it becomes clearer how the cold water temperature markedly changes throughout the year. It's usually in October when you feel the water temperature get much colder. There's like a day when that happens, when the water feels more biting than it did the day before. It's a really wonderful experience.

Today, that happened in the other direction. Today it felt like the water was less biting than it was yesterday.

There are other changes. My sourdough bread, which I make about five days a week, started to rise much more quickly than it did throughout the winter. This is even though the temperature inside the house is not really much different than it was when I had the heater on.

We're technically a couple of weeks away from the start of spring, but I like how I feel how seasons change differently and more personally through these experiences.

resize

March 05, 2024

I use the alacritty terminal. I can zoom in and out in alacritty (change size) like other terminals with ctrl +/-.

However, I'd mostly rather change the font size directly. To do that, you edit the $HOME/.config/alacritty/alacritty.yml configuration file. For example, to change from a 13 point font size to a 14 point font size, you change 13 to 14 in the config file: size: 14 from size: 13.

Rather than open that configuration file, I wrote a little bash script to make it easier:

#!/bin/bash

resize() {
  ptsize="$1"
  config="$HOME/.config/alacritty/alacritty.yml"
  sed -i "/size: /s/[0-9][0-9]/$ptsize/" "$config"
}

resize "$@"

Now, to do something like change the font size to a 14 point, I only have to type something like:

resize 14

syslog

February 22, 2024

Last night stuff on my computer started to break in a weird way. The laptop fan started to run at full blast right as I was walking away from it to take my children some where. I didn't think anything of it, but when we returned home four hours later, the fan was still running.

After investigating for a bit, I found that Firefox was using over a 100% of a CPU core and that /var/log/syslog had grown to a 120G with errors related to Firefox on Wayland.

I cleared the syslog file with:

sudo truncate -s 0 /var/log/syslog

And then edited /etc/logrotate.d/rsyslog to prevent the syslog file from taking up so much space in the future. I also found that someone had submitted a bug report on this exact problem to launchpad.net. I added a confirmation to the bug report.

In the meantime, I'm switching to another browser until this is resolved. This is unrelated to the bug, but I prefer Firefox and yet it's becoming increasingly clear that this is a Chrome-based world. I'm starting to think that I might have to make a permanent switch because many websites that I use for work are not as functional with Firefox as they are with Chrome-based browsers. I dislike that, a lot.

Breathe and Sleep

February 19, 2024

My daily breath work exercises include coherent breathing, 4-7-8 breathing, and Hof breathing, and they've all done me wonders in their own ways. I credit these excersises and daily cold water immersion with avoiding getting sick, despite living with three children, all of whom have had a rotation of the regular illnesses, since October 2021.

However, I sleep terribly. There have been a couple of times since October 2021 that I felt like I was going to be unwell. Even though they were momentary, and I always ended up feeling fine after a short bit and some breathwork, each time directly correlated after a string of nights of very poor sleep (like three to four hours of restless sleep each night for several nights in a row).

Last week I had a medical checkup, and we discussed this sleep problem. Instead of medication, which I didn't want, I was encouraged to keep a sleep diary to help me focus on my sleep hygiene. I think this is a great idea. I'm going to get a grip on this and learn how to sleep well in the coming weeks and months.

Alternate Computer History

January 29, 2024

I like to imagine a world where computing developed differently.

In this world, there would be a focus on a static web and on command line applications. We would spend more time learning about our computers rather than engaging the web. Computing would prioritize simplicity and efficiency. We would acquire a different form of digital literacy in this world.

Command line skills would be more important in this world. We would start early in developing skills with scripting languages.

The web would be composed of static pages and would prioritize content over form. Websites would serve more as repositories of information rather than the dynamic, interactive, and bloated platforms of today.

Digital aesthetics would lean towards minimalism and functionality. My text based web browser would excel in this world. Hardware would become obsolete at a much slower pace.

Social interactions on the web or the internet would occur through forums, chat rooms, and BBSes. This would change the social nature of the web or the internet, and may even be more slow-paced.

Computer resources could be devoted to other tasks rather than rendering bloated JavaScript websites.

Education in computing would be more rooted in understanding the underlying OS through the command line. There would be, I imagine, a stronger emphasis on computational thinking and problem-solving. Perhaps this would result in a different approach to teaching programming and computing in general.

Screen Recording

December 05, 2023

I need screen casting software to record videos for my classes. The problem is that I use the Sway window manager and Wayland, but ever since the switch from i3 and X11, I have had trouble finding good screen recording software. OBS Studio is great, but it often crashes in Sway (but not in Gnome).

I'd prefer to stay in Sway, and I found wf-recorder, which works great. However, sometimes I need to pause the video when I am recording. Since wf-recorder is a commandline program, I can't just hit the pause button.

But, ctrl-z works perfectly! When I'm ready to unpause, I just type fg to bring it back up.

I invoke wf-recorder as screen_rec, which saves the recording in my home Videos directory, screen_rec is called by the following function in my .bash_functions file:

screen_rec() {
    if [ -x "$(command -v wf-recorder)" ] ; then
        cd "$HOME/Videos" || { echo "Videos directory not found"; return; }
        wf-recorder --audio
    else
        echo "wf-recorder not found or not executable."
        return
    fi
}

Learning and Identity

October 20, 2023

People really want to say that they are X kind of learner. That learning style mumbo jumbo really sticks. Winter Bread

October 09, 2023

Last winter when I was baking my bread, I'd make the dough in the morning, and let it rise all day. Before bed, I'd shape the loaf, and place it in a cast iron dutch oven. I'd place the dutch oven in the fridge for the night. I'd leave the starter out overnight, too, so that it would be ready in the morning. I'd pull the dough out of the fridge in the morning, and bake it.

When the weather warmed up last spring, I had to change the timing. I started the dough later in the morning or early in the afternoon. The starter would not be pulled out of the fridge till the morning, either. The warmer weather simply changed the entire workflow.

The weather is cooling, and it took me a couple of days to recall that bread making is seasonal, too.

The Accused

September 15, 2023

The other day I took my children to their ballet lessons. I had all of them, but only the older ones had lessons that evening. I prepared dinner ahead of time, to eat between lessons, and since it was a very nice evening, the youngest one and I headed out to the van to eat.

The van back door is wide open, and the little one is eating in the back of the van and we're chatting. There are people in the car parked next to us, and they are getting ready to leave. Another car pulls up and blocks our cars. This new car has tinted windows, so I can't see inside.

At first I think that the person in the new arrival is about to turn into a space, but they just idle there, primarily behind my car but far enough ahead to block the other people. I get a bit nervous, because there are lots of spaces in the lot, and it makes me wonder what their intent is.

Finally they roll their passenger window down. There's a woman. She starts yelling at me.

What are you doing with that kid?

What do you mean what am I doing with this kid?

I said, what are you doing with that kid?

What do you mean?

Her voice gets louder and her face angier:

What are you doing with that kid?

What? Mind your own business.

It's my business if you're doing something wrong with that kid.

I'm her Dad. Drive on.

She glared at me for a minute. The people parked next to us were waiting for her to move out of the way. Finally she drove to the other end of the lot, rolled her window down, and watched us. I took my child into the studio, and we waited there for the rest of the evening till we could go home.

A Delight

August 23, 2023

The Book of Delights by Ross Gay is a wonderful read.

Perhaps the book placed me in a good frame of mind when a friend texted me that their friend was going to be at the art fair down the street last weekend and was texting me, and not the friend, because the friend's phone wasn't working. There were like thousands of people at the fair, and I thought, without their phone, how would I find them? And the thought of looking for them, with my own children in tow, amidst that sea of people, made me laugh with delight.

On Hold

August 18, 2023

This is the process. I review a list of recommended or best of books on some website. I look the ebook up using my public library. It's inevitably checked out to someone else. Put book on hold and search for another book that was listed on that some website. It's available. I check it out, but I don't read it quickly enough. The book on hold becomes available, and since I'm facing a due date on the current one, I select 'deliver later'. By the time I am ready for the next book, I can't remember how I learned about it. It becomes like a surprise book.

Sourdough Bread

August 03, 2023

I created my first sourdough started in 2001 when I worked at a small cafe. I remember it being very delicious and used it to make all sorts of bread for the restaurant. It lasted less than a year, until I moved on to a new restaurant far, far away.

Last summer, because my children goes through so much bread, I decided to create a new starter and bake bread again to save some dough (ahem) and just to have really delicious bread. It took a couple of months to get the baking process nailed down, but it's turned out great. The crust is superb, dark, chewy, and the sourness comes through sharply but not overly so. The house smells great every morning, which is when I normally bake it. I'm making a loaf about five or so days a week, which is fine because it means I don't have to discard starter ever.

Oftentimes, five or so loaves a week is too much, but that's okay. I like give the extra loaf to a friend or colleague. Yesterday I knew I'd have an extra loaf today, and so I took today's loaf to my neighbor. I really hope they enjoy it. They seemed really excited to get it, which made really happy. It's like my favorite gift to give.

Automake

July 10, 2023

I use a set of scripts to automate publishing to this page. For fun, I wrote the scripts using a lot of ed commands, and now I want to update the scripts using a more manageable language and workflow.

I started reworking the scripts by asking ChatGPT to create a Python script to handle the process. The code it produced looked fine, on the surface, but I didn't give it a chance. Instead I thought I'd implement the existing Bash scripts with a Makefile. I described the list of scripts that I used to ChatGPT and asked it to create a Makefile for me. We went through several iterations, but it didn't pan out. To get a functional Makefile working, I realized that I would need to change how the project folders are organized and change how variables and arguments are used in the Bash scripts. As an aside, ChatGPT highly discouraged me from using ed commands (haha).

In the end, I started rewriting the scripts using mostly sed commands. There's still some ed commands to scrub, but when I'm done, I should be able to write a simple Bash container script that will call the other scripts as dependencies.

In the end, I asked ChatGPT 25 questions for this exercise. Even though it didn't pan out, it's a far more enjoyable process than conducting a bunch of web searches.

Plain Text Research

March 18, 2022

I submitted a juried paper proposal to the ALISE 2022 annual conference on the topic of ungrading. Since this is a solo project, I was able to use a plain text research workflow. Essentially, I wrote the paper in markdown, added citations using the ZotCite plugin for Vim, versioned the file with Git and added it to a GitHub repo. It was a really enjoyable process.

When the paper was about ready, I used my Bash function to convert the file to an ODT file (see first link above) following the APA style guide. I made some minor style edits to conform to the conference guidelines for paper proposals and removed some library proxy URLs. Then I submitted it as a PDF file, which I exported from LibreOffice.

I also converted the file to an HTML file: Ungrade to Learn.

On Sex, Politics, and Religion

March 17, 2022

Sometime in the early 1990s, my religious studies professor lamented that we, as a society, avoided discussions on sex, politics, and religion. I think he was sad that we didn't talk about these topics because we didn't know how to talk about them. I agree. Today these topics seem to be all that we talk about, even though we seemed to not have learned how to talk about them.

731 Days

March 14, 2022

Future Updates

I'll keep adding memories to this post as they come to me.

Updated on March 18, 2022.

March 13, 2020 was 731 days ago. Today marks two years since the first day of quarantine.

I mainly remember the faculty meetings we had on the 13th. That means I'll always remember that it was a Friday.

I remember thinking this would be a two-year event simply because the 1918 flu pandemic lasted about two years. But I really had no way of knowing.

I read today that another wave might be forming in Europe. If so, I believe this would be the sixth wave.

We're largely behaving like it's pre-pandemic days now, and that might be okay for most of us who have taken advantage of those tools that reduce our risk and are fortunate to have those tools available to us. But it's still a hazard for many in the world.

I really don't want to forget how surreal it felt then, before we knew the Illness was transmitted through the air rather than via surfaces.

And how sad it was after quarantine time kept going on and on, for my family, and for others too, especially those who lost people.

I remember the Governor's daily briefings. I kept my own log of deaths and hospitalizations, emailed from his office, in order to examine the data myself. But it wasn't eye opening. The data seemed too messy. Instead, it was just tragic, all those deaths and hospitalizations.

My youngest was still a baby when the pandemic struck, but they were eventually full toddler and walking and being toddler funny within a year. I was able to witness that for my two older children but probably way more so with the youngest.

There were momemts of joy. The streets were void of activity. Like liminal spaces. But that made them fun to take my young children on bike rides around town. It felt safe but also grand, and my second oldest learned to ride during this time period.

We've long cut our own hair in our household, but in those early months, we made some bold choices with our hair styles.

The world feels like it's falling apart still, but I also like to remember how supportive people seemed to be of each other in those first few months. It didn't take long before it was all politicized, though. I don't miss that.

I remember there was loads of fear, and that for some, there still is. The other day I was walking home. A man was walking in my direction. He was wearing a mask. As we approached to pass each other, he scooted so far from me that he almost fell into the bush adjacent to the sidewalk. I was sad for his trauma.

Embracing the Cold Front

March 12, 2022

As usual for early March, we received some snow after a couple of weeks of spring-like weather. I took advantage of it and started Saturday and Sunday mornings with seven and six rounds, respectively, of Wim Hof breathing. I usually only have time for three rounds during the week, so it was nice to go longer and deeper. I wanted to go seven this morning, but six was hard to complete because the little one kept jumping on my belly.

Afterwards I went in the backyard wearing only a pair of shorts and flip flops, and stood there for ten minutes. It was 21 degrees on Saturday and 26 on Sunday morning, but seven degrees with windchill on both days. When ten minutes passed, I went inside and took long ice cold showers, which felt warm more quickly than usual, and finished with hot water, which felt kinda okay but not as fantastic as I anticipated.

Months ago I got to the point where I thought I enjoyed cold showers more than hot showers, and somehow, in my experience, hot showers started to feel a bit gross and slimy. But it's still a mental effort to make the switch permanent. My brain still says that I don't want to be in the cold, even though it turns out great every single time.

How Long

March 7, 2022

Let's say you're visiting a friend, and you spend a few hours at their place. It's a good visit, but you forgot something in your car. You say, "be right back," then you run out to the car, and you re-enter the house without knocking. That should be fine, right?

Or, let's say it's someone close that you're visiting. It is, in fact, someone you visit quite often, maybe even a family member of some sorts. But otherwise, it's the same scenario: You forgot something, get it, and return and re-enter the house without knocking. That should still be fine, I would think.

In any kind of situation like this, how long can you leave before you have to knock again? Or is it not (only) a matter of time but also of the in-between events that take place? Like the things that may happen between stepping out and returning? Or is it whether there is closure when stepping out?

Anyway, these are rhetorical questions, but they are ones I feel like I'm facing, albeit in a much more abstract way.

Posting Here

February 11, 2022

I've blogged off and on since blogging was a thing, but it never stuck. I think that was because I've despised every blogging platform that I've ever used. The platform matters for such things. The closest I came to liking a blogging platform was when I used Octopress to log my reading of The Library Quarterly. Octopress was fun to use for a while, but as the posts accumulated, it became annoying to rebuild it every time I added more. So I put all those posts on my wiki and gave up blogging for the nth time.

Even though I didn't stick with blogging because I disliked the available platforms, in my moments of doubt, I wondered if my failure to keep going indicated that I didn't have a voice, or something to say. That really bothered me at times. But of course I have a voice, it was simply that the toolchains and the platforms I used never matched my voice. This toolchain and this platform I've created to post here are my expressions, too. I think that is why I write here about the process of automating this site. It was as important to develop the site to post here as it was to post here for me to keep posting here. Haha!

But even so, I created this page about a year before the March 2020 lockdown, but I only added 16 posts to this page in that first year. In the last two years since lockdown, I've added 140 more, or about 4 posts per month in 2020 and about 7 posts per month in 2021. I don't know what that means. Except to think that, besides the pandemic, other big things happened in those last two years. I don't write about them here. A vast majority of what I put on this page is superficial, I guess. But I think, maybe, it all left me a bit reflective.

On Trust

February 9, 2022

I gave the toddler a bath last night, and as usual, when I asked her to lay her head back, she was scared to go under the water. I've taught the two older children how to swim, and it was the same in that setting, too. They have all, at first and for a while, been scared to put their heads under the water. And this is despite that I hold them all when they take a bath or learn to swim.

This fear struck me as odd because when they are scared of something in the world, they will come and hold and hide behind my legs. So I had thought that they see me as someone who would protect them. Who they trust themselves to. But this water thing made me realize that, even though I am their father, that trust is not absolute. That their fear of going under the water is greater than their trust in me to protect them. Instinctually, at least.

When it first happened, with my oldest, and then my middle child, I have to admit, I felt a little hurt. I thought, "why doesn't this child trust me completely?" But over time, it made me, not happy really, but in awe perhaps, that I have to earn their trust. And I felt proud that it wasn't given to me completely without having to earn it.

This lesson made me realize something about earning trust among other people, too. In fact, I don't think I ever understood trust before becoming a parent for a long while. Maybe not even until very recently.

Automating This Site

February 5, 2022

I've added a page to this site detailing how I post to this page using a series of bash scripts.

I wrote the scripts to convert the original markdown file into an HTML file, modify the HTML file in specific ways, insert the HTML file into this page, create a table of contents item for this page and the homepage, create a RSS entry, insert that entry into the index.xml file, and then deploy the site to the remote server.

It's nice to have this all done, but I'm sure I'll keep polishing these scripts.

Re-reading Thomas Merton

February 4, 2022

About thirty years ago, I devoured Thomas Merton's works. I even wrote a (bad) thesis on him as a student fellow at the Newberry Library in Chicago. But I haven't read or re-read anything of his since the mid to late 1990s. I tried some years ago, soon after we moved to Kentucky. I thought being in Kentucky, not far from his abbey, would be inspiring. But I couldn't get back into it.

I don't know why his writings became so inaccessible to me. Why does that happen? Why does a book or a thought or a way of thinking become distant? In this case, I don't think it has anything to do with Merton. I think something changed with me around the turn of the century. (I have some ideas about what happened.)

Maybe I'm ready this time. It feels like it's getting closer.

Obsession

January 28, 2022

A couple of days ago I posted here that I had nearly automated the site. If you are reading this post, then it means that I've fully automated the site. I think this is called obsessing. Anyways, now I can write a post in markdown, and then run some commands to process the site and copy it to the remote server.

My approach has me cracking up a bit because the scripts (all five of them plus two functions stored in my .bash_functions file) are pretty ridiculous in some ways. Most of the code draws upon the following:

  • ed
  • sed
  • various bash utilities

All of this would likely have been a lot easier if I had used JavaScript in a lot of places, but I really, really wanted to use ed(1) as much as possible, and I really, really want to avoid using JavaScript on this site. It was tricky, too, because I had to use ed(1) to insert text in the middle of lines. Since ed(1) is a line editor, that's not something it was really meant to do. But I did it, and I learned a lot.

Here's an example script that primarily uses ed(1) to add additional HTML elements, attributes, and values to an existing HTML file:

#!/usr/bin/bash

# Original date: 2022-01-28

# After running ``makepost`` (in .bash_functions), which will convert the
# markdown file into an HTML one. This ed(1) commands below add some additional
# HTML elements etc to make the post ready for the main index.html file.

# Usage: ./make-article post-name.html

post="$1"

ed -s "$post" << 'EOF'
0i
<article id="
.
1r !date +\%Y\%m\%d
1,2j
1a
">
.
1,2j
3s/[0-9]\">/\
&\

.
4r !date +\%Y-\%m-\%d
4a
<time datetime="
.
6a
">
.
8s/<\/h3>/<\/time><\/h3>
3,8j
$a
</article>

.
wq
EOF

The code is super specific to my site, but I could probably generalize it someday as a flat-file blogging application and then make it available as an open source project.

I'll write a separate page about the scripts and post a link to that page on my homepage, which needs more clutter.

New Site Redesign

January 26, 2022

It was time for a site redesign. I'm teaching a web development course this semester, and working on that course inspired me to do something fun with this site. It may be less attractive than the prior version, but I like the busy-ness of this new look. In fact, the goal for this current version was to mimic, at least on the front page, those old Pennysaver-like ad rags that were fun to skim through. I think those things were fun, in part, because they contained so little white space. They screamed abundance. I plan to add more on the front page to fully realize that look.

I also updated my automation scripts (see GitHub repo) for this site:

I updated the WWW-toc-generator script because I have a table of contents in two locations now: the home page and this page. The script therefore needed to insert the most recent entry in both places.

In the prior version of the make-rss script, I used the xmlstarlet program to parse and extract values for the RSS feed from this index.html file. It's bothered me to depend on xmlstarlet. It's a nice program, but I wanted to rely on the fundamentals. So I finally did some digging and figured out a way to use GNU grep to replace it. make-rss is still quite a wonky script, but it's more interesting now, I think.

Finally, I crossed off a big item of my to-do list. In the past, my make-rss script would simply generate the XML I needed for my index.xml (RSS) file and store it in a temporary file. To use that XML, I'd manually insert it into the proper places in index.xml. I wrote a script, insert-xml that would automate that process. The script primarily contains ed(1) commands.

Since I write these posts in separate, markdown files, the last item to accomplish before this site is fully automated is to write a script that will insert new posts in the proper place of this index.html file. Once I get around to that, whenever I write a new post, I will be able to type one command to process the site and copy it to the remote server. Almost there!

High School

January 18, 2022

The other day I reunited with one of my closest friends from high school and also my favorite high school teacher, and it was marvelous. My former teacher gave me a top ten lifetime hug, and I cried a bit.

My former teacher is now 80 years old and is as sharp as she was 31 years ago. She remembered my Mom's name, my siblings' names (even though they never had her as a teacher). She recounted old stories, some of which I had forgotten.

She told me that, not too many years ago, she and her partner were once driving through Illinois when she saw a sign pointing to Monmouth, and she told her partner to take a detour there. She wanted to see where I went to college. She told me that she saw the building where I worked my first year as, among all things, a boiler room attendant. That I had such a job made her laugh. My task was to sit in this boiler room in a football field size basement on Friday nights from 11pm to 7am, and watch the pressure gauges. If the pressure got too high, it meant a boiler was going to explode and I was supposed to call someone. I had homework and a 7-inch black and white TV that picked up one station, but mostly static, to keep me occupied. I'd watch Friday Night Videos and nod off to sleep when the station signed off for the night.

I went to college, and that specific college, because of that teacher. One of my favorite authors is someone she told me I would want to read. I was in the speech club because of her, and on the debate team. She was a critical reader of my homework assignments. In 1987 she taught me my first lesson: not to spell "a lot" like "alot". I don't know why that memory stuck, but to this day I can't write "a lot" without thinking of her.

We talked about that time period and the people then. We talked about all the debate tournament shenanigans we caused in protest of the new trend in speed reading (spreading). It didn't help calm our passions that the teams employing this distasteful practice came from the wealthy schools. I once walked out of a debate because of that foolishness. She remembered it all, and we laughed.

She created those kinds of opportunities and made those kinds of introductions for me and for countless other students during her career, including my close, beautiful friend who arranged the reunion. We all came from pretty humble, and sometimes frightening or challenging backgrounds. It was a very marginalized area of San Antonio, but I couldn't tell you how many PhDs, MDs, and other teachers came out of my class alone, because of her and a few other amazing teachers. I couldn't imagine how many lives she made better in her decades long career. People love her. I do, too. A lot.

Thank you for being in my life and for shaping so much of it, Ms. Bailey.

Reading Patterns

January 11, 2022

I've noticed that I do not like to read nonfiction lately. My last book before the new year was a work of fiction, and I finished it fairly quickly. Then I picked up a work of nonfiction, and I barely touch the book even though it's a good book by someone I really like.

The last time this happened I had to abandon the nonfiction work and take up a work of fiction before I started to read consistently again. In fact, this has happened several times. It's been hard to get through nonfiction.

Maybe I just want stories. I stopped reading fiction for a few years, and maybe I starved myself of something by doing that. I'm enjoying the change, though. Feels like my old, pre-grad school self in some ways, when I used to read a lot of fiction.

Semantic Line Breaks

January 9, 2022

I learned about semantic line breaks today. This might be a game-changer for me and my use of the ed(1) text editor, along with a markup language like markdown. See the link to read more.

The goal with using semantic line breaks is to make it easier to revise text with ed(1) (or any other editor) since it means using easier commands to edit the text.

I first learned about using shorter input lines a few months ago when I was reading about troff. But I was visually turned off from the idea because the examples I saw were difficult to read. Using semantic line breaks should solve that problem. I am using them now to write this post, and I can tell it will take a while to get used to but that it might be worth it to explore.

On Papers as Assignments

December 13, 2021

I don't ask students to write essays or papers in my undergraduate classes, and that's mostly because they are not those kinds of classes.

The graduate courses I teach are those kinds of classes, but I want to think about them differently. So, in addition to ungrading, I'm going to start replacing paper assignments with other kinds of assignments in those classes. I had implemented this kind of change a while back but not fully. I have some ideas about what to do instead of papers, and I'm looking forward to seeing how it'll play out next semester.

I also am completely rethinking the undergraduate class I teach in the spring. I meant to have that revision done way before now, but it looks like I may not get it done until right before the semester starts.

As an aside, I can't believe it's almost 2022. That seems weird.

On Smiling

November 23, 2021

Someone smiled at me yesterday as they waited on me to finish parking my car so that they could get to theirs, which was adjacent to ours. When they smiled, I gotta tell you, there was something about it that was so unbelievably pure and good. It just lit me up and filled me with great joy for a moment.

This is the third time I've seen this kind of smile in my life from a complete stranger. The last time was over 20 years ago. Like those, I'll probably remember this one for a long time. Thank you whoever you are, and I hope you smile like that for always.

Breakthrough Case Update, Day 10

November 12, 2021

Feeling much better. Still have a bit of a cough, but it feels like it's lessening. Last time I posted, I felt the cough becoming a chest cough, and I wrote how I re-engaged with the breathing technique that I'm following. It seemed to do the trick. The cough feels like it's due to a nasal drip, and it does not feel like it's migrating down to my chest. In the past, when I've had coughs become chest coughs, it would take forever to heal. So things appear to be looking up.

By the way, I meant to add this last time, but my sense of smell started to return at about day 7 or so.

Breakthrough Case Update, Day 6

November 8, 2021

Felt very tired today. Had a lot of work to do, too. Got through it. Slept for a little bit in the evening, which is why I'm still awake at 11:39pm. I'd like to be asleep now, but I'm thankful for the short nap. It helped improve my mood and sense of well being quite a bit. I was feeling rather depressed, in fact.

Cough has moved down to the chest. I hate that so much. I thought that the breathing exercises should have helped prevent this somewhat, and I've kept up with those exercises and cold showers. But I also felt like I had gone off track with the former. It felt like I wasn't doing them right. I wasn't getting the high-ish feeling nor was my breath retention long enough. I also wasn't relaxing when doing them. Could never not feel stressed.

So tonight I re-watched one of the how-to videos that Hof has on YT, and followed the instructions to the letter. Two things happened. First, it confirmed that I had gone off track. I wasn't relaxing enough nor was I really breathing in well enough. The video helped guide me back, and correcting these two things enabled me to lengthen my breath retention considerably. Second, after I re-learned the lesson and completed four rounds, my cough subsided substantially. I'll repeat the process in the morning before my next cold shower. If I ever feel like I'm going off track again, I can re-watch those videos.

Groff and Troff

October 30, 2021

I started learning LaTeX a long time ago, within the first few years of using Linux. I liked learning LaTeX, but because I wasn't in school or higher education at the time, I mostly used it to create silly documents. When I did go to graduate school, I used it for it's intended purpose, which was to typeset papers. I also used it to markup notes in some classes, like my ANOVA class. As a result, I have ANOVA notes that look very nice and are very readable, I think.

It must have been around that time that I learned about groff, troff, and nroff, the document typesetting utilities that have been with Unix since the earliest of their days (well, groff is a GNU addition that came much later), but I didn't learn them at the time.

I already don't remember what motivated me, but I started to learn groff and the groff_mm macro a couple of weeks ago, and I love it. I mailed a letter to a friend the other day, and I used the man 7 groff_mm macro. This is a bit of what the source code looks like:

\" This is a comment
\" Set Writer Address
.WA "Sean"
Street Address
City, State ZIP
.WE
\" Set Addressee Information
.IA
Addressee
Street Address
City, State ZIP
.IE
\" Set salutation
.LO SA "Dear Addressee,"
\" Format letter as Semi-Blocked
.LT SB
.P
This is a paragraph.
\" Add an italic to a word in another paragraph
.P This is another
.I
paragraph.
.R
\" Set closing
.FC
\" Insert Signature Line
.SG

There's a lot more to learn, and I think I'll keep exploring. The system is very Unix-y, and I find that appealing.

On Ungrading and Learning

October 19, 2021

I have fully embraced ungrading this semester.

This has been my approach so far:

  1. Assignments have due dates, but these are flexible. If students get behind, I make a point to communicate with them often. Regardless of how flexible my policy is, we still have an end to the semester.
  2. I mark work as Complete (100%) or Incomplete (0%). Incomplete work is work that is unsatisfactory per the assignment requirements or work that hasn't been completed by the due date. In either Incomplete case, students can still submit or they can resubmit after revision. I encourage resubmission.
  3. I provide lots of feedback on all assignments.
  4. Final grades are calculated as the total completed work divided by the total work assigned, and also weighted by assignment type. This allows students to skip some (but not much) work and still do well in the course.

The process has really invigorated me, and I think it's been good for the students, too. And that's not just because I've removed grades but because I've forced revisions. I think I'm also getting quite a bit more responses from students to my feedback, and that's been super cool and interesting because it's spawned some conversations after completion.

The goal with all this is to center learning and decenter grading. Let's see how it continues to go.

Some Automation

October 6, 2021

I have an installation of Omeka that I manage for some colleagues who use it for a course they teach, but it's on a server that can't do email (long story, and I can work around it, but I don't want to). So the challenge has been how to create 50 to 60 accounts for students at a time. But because I used Omeka on a project a long time ago (the site is still running, but the link goes to a 2008 version, which is about the time we launched it), I knew that I could work with the MySQL database it uses. And so a couple of years ago I wrote a small shell script to help me generate SQL statements that I could use to create accounts. This saved having to do it manually from the Omeka admin interface.

However, I had only partially automated the process. It saved a lot of grunt work and time, but I still had to do some of the work manually (hey, it's not my full time job!). So each semester, I'd tackle a little bit more of that task, and I'm glad to say that I have it fully automated. After clearing out the previous semester's content and accounts in the MySQL database, all I have to do is download the course rosters, which are exported from my university as XLSX files, save those as CSV files, and then run the script. The script then generates three files of SQL statements. One SQL file creates the accounts, the second one updates the tables with salted passwords, and the third adds the salts for the passwords. Then I can source those files after logging into the MySQL database. The code is one of my GitHub repos.

It's an early version of the script, and I'm sure I'll come across a bug or two eventually, but it works perfectly now. The main bug I'll watch for is how it handles variations with student names. I think it's robust against that, but there are times when students have "Jr.", "III", or four or more names, and I'll need to watch for how it handles those. I also want to make it function-based too, which will make it easier to maintain.

I've had this on the back burner for a while, and it feels good to be pretty much done with it, maintenance and tweaks aside.

OpenURL

October 1, 2021

My university's library uses OpenURL and Primo/Alma's discovery service/link resolver to request sources via various bibliographic databases. Or more accurately, the university library uses Primo/Alma, which uses an OpenURL compatible link resolver.

Basically, the technology is meant to help library users gain access to a library's (usually paywalled) electronic resources (or locate items in the catalog). E.g., if you're searching on Google Scholar and you see a View Now @ [YOUR LIBRARY] link next to a search result, and you click on that link, then you are using, or triggering, a link resolver to request the source. As a library user, you have to add your institution in Google Scholar via their settings, but if you're using a bibliographic or citation database directly provided by your library, then it should be available by default.

Because OpenURL compatible link resolver technology is partly based on query strings, it's fun to glean all sorts of information by examining the URL: the query string component that contains the metadata for the source but also the base component that contains the vendor and institutional information and also the URL type. Namely, it turns out that Primo/Alma uses two URL types to request resources: a search URL and an OpenURL. At my institution, the base search URL looks like this:

https://saalck-uky.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/search?

The base OpenURL differs just a bit (see the end of the URL):

https://saalck-uky.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?

The base search URL appears when searching the university's online catalog (or discovery service). However, the OpenURL appears only in transit between the original resource and before reaching the final source; e.g., after clicking on a View Now @ link and before being redirected to the full text version of the article that might be available at the original location (an open access location) or via the proxy location (e.g., EZproxy). Because the redirect happens quickly, I had to quickly copy my institution's specific OpenURL when I clicked on a View Now link before it redirected to the EZproxy page.

My students often identify great problems to solve or are the source of great ideas. One of my students in my electronic resource management class noticed that Zotero has a locate menu that uses OpenURL resolvers to look up items in a library. By default, Zotero uses WorldCat, but it can use a specific institution's OpenURL resolver, too. I had completely forgotten about this. I think I stopped trying to use it years ago because the WorldCat default stopped working. Anyway, when I investigated, I noticed that my institution wasn't available via Zotero or listed on their page of OpenURL resolvers.

At the time, I didn't know what my institution's exact OpenURL was, but I was able to figure it out by comparing the syntax and values from other Primo URLs listed on Zotero's page of OpenURL resolvers and because I had just written a lecture on link resolvers. So by comparing OpenURLs and by using the info from my lecture, where I had discussed the components of link resolver URLs, I was able to derive my institution's specific OpenURL (base component plus institutional info), which is:

https://saalck-uky.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01SAA_UKY&vid=01SAA_UKY:UKY

I added that to Zotero, and it worked, and then I posted the OpenURL info to Zotero's forum, and they've added it to their OpenURL resolver page. That's pretty cool.

If anyone who comes across this post and is curious about how to add this info to Zotero, another library has created a video on this. The directions cover adding a specific OpenURL to Zotero and on how to use Zotero's Library Lookup functionality.

My students are awesome, and I thank them for challenging me.

Vim, Ed, and also Markdown

September 13, 2021

Some things I like doing in /usr/bin/vim, /usr/bin/nvi, or /usr/bin/ed:

Use the % (percent sign) to run a sh command on the current buffer.

For example, from within Vim or ed, I can convert a markdown file to an HTML (or whatever else pandoc supports):

# save file; run pandoc from sh, output markdown file to a file named file.html
w !pandoc % -o test.html

# view the file with the w3m text browser
!w3m test.html

This behavior is buried in the info ed documentation. Vim inherits this behavior as a descendant of ed, but I first learned about it in a tweet. From info ed:

an unescaped '%' is replaced by the default filename

More often when I'm generating an HTML file from a markdown file, which I do a lot because I write lectures and other content in markdown, I use a function that I wrote and keep in my .bash_functions file. I like this function because it keeps smart formatting out of my HTML.

makepost () {
  sourcefile="$1"
  pandoc -f gfm -t html "$sourcefile" > \
    $(basename -s md "$sourcefile")html
}

Alternatively, I could do this within ed(1) or Vim. In command mode:

w !pandoc -f gfm -t html % > somefile.html

When I exit the editor, I can do makepost somefile.md to generate somefile.html. To copy that file from the command line, I use the following function that copies the contents of a text file to my clipboard. Then I can paste that (via Ctrl-v) in Canvas or wherever I want the HTML:

clip () {
  xclip -selection clipboard -i "$1"
}

To use that, I do clip somefile.html.

I use /usr/bin/ed quite often. I've found that it's a great editor for writing because I find it easier to simply stay in insert mode longer (i.e., Vim, as a screen editor, faciliates mode switching). I have also found that ed(1) is great for editing because it helps me focus on one line at a time. I'll usually go back to Vim when I want to make some big passes through a work.

Measuring Food with Pandas

August 16, 2021

I wanted to reproduce in pandas and Python some of what I did with my tortillas post a couple of months ago, when I ran a small test in R to see how consistent I was measuring dough for tortillas. I'm doing this to learn pandas better.

Pandas has additional data objects or structures, and they each have distinct attributes and methods. This is something that I've had to learn coming from R; specifically, that R is more like pandas than it is like Python. Here are some tables comparing pandas and R.

I can use NumPy with default objects like lists and tuples, but there are three pandas specific data structures. The first two are the main structures for analyzing data:

  • Series, like R vectors
  • DataFrame, like R dataframes
  • Index

To get started:

import pandas as pd
import numpy as np

# create data as *list*
tortillas = [2.1, 2.0, 1.9, 1.7, 2.3, 2.2, 2.0, 2.4, 2.1, 1.9, 2.1, 2.3, 2.0, 2.4]

# get basic stats on *list*
np.mean(tortillas)
np.median(tortillas)
np.max(tortillas) - np.min(tortillas)
np.std(tortillas)
round(np.std(tortillas), 2)

# get basic stats on list converted to pandas Series
tort2 = pd.Series(tortillas, copy = True)
pd.Series.mean(tort2)
pd.Series.median(tort2)
pd.Series.mode(tort2)
pd.Series.max(tort2) - pd.Series.min(tort2)
pd.Series.describe(tort2)

# However, here's the shorthand:
tort2.describe()
tort2.le(2.0) # return Booleans less than 2.0

To perform a t test, we need another library, and here SciPy is common. For a one sample t test, I wanted to know if the observed mean was different from a mean of 2.0, which is what I was aiming to get when I was measuring out the dough:

from scipy.stats import ttest_1samp

# works on pd.Series or Python Lists
ttest_1samp(tort2, 2.0)
ttest_1samp(tortillas, 2.0)
# These results agree with my R results:
Ttest_1sampResult(statistic=1.8358568490953695, pvalue=0.08934987597048166)
# Save the results
tscore, pvalue = ttest_1samp(tort2, 2.0)
print('t test equals: ', round(tscore, 3))
print('p value equals: ', round(pvalue, 3))

It seems that I'd have to calculate the confidence intervals myself if I only used SciPy, but there's a library called pingouin that can report the important details for the test:

import pingouin as pg
pg.ttest(tort2, 2.0)
               T  dof alternative    p-val         CI95%   cohen-d   BF10     power
T-test  1.835857   13   two-sided  0.08935  [1.98, 2.22]  0.490653  1.015  0.397749

And the results are the same for the R version of this.

diff, ed, patch

August 13, 2021

Sometimes I like to do things using the old ways, largely because it’s sometimes interesting how thing were done a while back. But also because even though I write some scripts in R and Python, I’m not any kind of software developer and have not had any real training in computer science.

This is all just to say that right now I’m learning a bit more about the diff command. I’ve used it quite a bit over the years to visually compare two or more text files, but I’ve never used it the way a programmer might use it; that is, by using it with the patch command or with an ed script. (I have, however, used RCS to edit web pages on a multi-user NetBSD based public access UNIX system. I remember how fun it was when I first had to use it back around 2004 or 2005.)

Anyways, to use ed and patch with diff, let’s say there are two files: file1.py file2.py, and file2.py is a modification of file1.py.

To merge them with diff and an ed script:

diff -e file1.py file2.py > patch.ed
(cat patch.ed && echo w) | ed - file1.py

Although I like to use ed a lot, it's probably easier to merge them with the diff and the patch commands:

diff -u file1.py file2.py > patchfile.patch
patch file1.py patchfile.patch

Updated: Sat May 31 11:09:00 AM EDT 2025

Vegetarian Red Bean Soup

August 10, 2021

Last night I made some pretty good vegetarian red bean soup. It’s pretty basic but delicious. Here is what I did:

Beans

Two cups dried red beans, soaked in salted water the night before.

After cooking the next day, I drained, rinsed, and set them aside.

Broth

I added some leftover vegetable scraps that I keep in the freezer to a large pot, along with a bit of rind from parmigiano reggiano, and added about two plus quarts of water. Brought this to a boil, and then let simmer for about an hour.

Soup

I chopped two small, yellow onions (or one big one would have worked), about half a bulb of garlic, and diced two red bell peppers.

I poured a good amount of olive oil along with a tablespoon or two of butter in a large soup pot. Turned the burner to medium-high, and when hot, added the onion.

Cooked the onion until just beginning to caramelize, and then added the red bell pepper, the garlic, and some salt. Cooked this until the red pepper was softened.

I added some dried seasonings, specifically some sofrito mix we get from TJs, and stirred this to release its fragrances.

Added the drained red beans, stirred, tasted, and added a bit more salt.

Then I poured the hot broth through a strainer and into the soup pot. I brought this up to a low boil but quickly turned it to a high simmer after it started to boil.

I let this simmer and meld for about 15 minutes, and then I used my handheld burr mixer and blended some of the soup. I stirred in a bunch of fresh spinach.

That was it. It was a nice soup.

We also had some leftover baked mahi mahi and some leftover soup, and today I added the fish to the soup and heated them up for lunch.
